# Searching Sourcegraph

Search before you build. Existing patterns reduce tokens, ensure consistency, and surface tested solutions.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Tool Selection Logic

**Start here:**

1. **Know the exact symbol or pattern?** → `sg_keyword_search`
2. **Know the concept, not the code?** → `sg_nls_search`
3. **Need to understand how/why?** → `sg_deepsearch_read`
4. **Tracing a symbol's usage?** → `sg_find_references`
5. **Need full implementation?** → `sg_go_to_definition` → `sg_read_file`

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Exact code patterns | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Trace usage | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Understand systems | `sg_deepsearch_read` |
| Read files | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
file:.*\.test\.ts$ -file:__mocks__   # Tests, exclude mocks
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

Combine filters: `repo:^github.com/myorg/backend$ file:src/handlers lang:typescript`

## Context-Aware Behaviour

**When the user provides a file path or error message:**
- Extract symbols, function names, or error codes
- Search for those exact terms first
- Trace references if the error involves a known symbol

**When the user asks "how does X work":**
- Prefer `sg_deepsearch_read` for architectural understanding
- Follow up with `sg_read_file` on key files mentioned in the response

**When the user is implementing a new feature:**
- Search for similar existing implementations first
- Read tests for usage examples
- Check for shared utilities before creating new ones

**When debugging:**
- Search for the exact error string
- Trace the symbol where the error is thrown
- Check recent changes with `sg_diff_search`

## Workflows

For detailed step-by-step workflows, see:
- `workflows/implementing-feature.md` — when building new features
- `workflows/understanding-code.md` — when exploring unfamiliar systems
- `workflows/debugging-issue.md` — when tracking down bugs

## Efficiency Rules

**Minimise tool calls:**
- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms (faster, more precise)

**Batch your understanding:**
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than reading one and asking questions
- Use `sg_deepsearch_read` for "how does X work" instead of multiple keyword searches

**Avoid common token waste:**
- Don't search all repos when you know the target repo
- Don't use `sg_deepsearch_read` for simple "find all" queries
- Don't re-read files you've already seen in this conversation

## Query Patterns

| Intent | Query |
|--------|-------|
| React hooks | `file:.*\.tsx$ use[A-Z].*= \(` |
| API routes | `file:src/api app\.(get\|post\|put\|delete)` |
| Error handling | `catch.*Error\|\.catch\(` |
| Type definitions | `file:types/ export (interface\|type)` |
| Test setup | `file:.*\.test\. beforeEach\|beforeAll` |
| Config files | `file:(webpack\|vite\|rollup)\.config` |
| CI/CD | `file:\.github/workflows deploy` |

For more patterns, see `query-patterns.md`.

## Output Formatting

**Search results:**
- Present as a brief summary, not raw tool output
- Highlight the most relevant file and line
- Include a code snippet only if it directly answers the question

**Code explanations:**
- Start with a one-sentence summary
- Use the codebase's own terminology
- Reference specific files and functions

**Recommendations:**
- Present as numbered steps if actionable
- Link to specific patterns found in the codebase
- Note any existing utilities that should be reused

## Common Mistakes

| Mistake | Fix |
|---------|-----|
| Searching all repos | Add `repo:^github.com/org/repo$` |
| Too many results | Add `file:` pattern or keywords |
| Missing relevant code | Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching |
| Not understanding context | Use `sg_deepsearch_read` |
| Guessing patterns | Read implementations with `sg_read_file` |

## Principles

- Start narrow, expand if needed
- Chain tools: search → read → find references → definition
- Check tests for usage examples
- Read before generating

# Task: Terraform v1.9.0 to v1.10.0 Migration Guide

## Objective

Analyze the changes between Terraform v1.9.0 and v1.10.0 and produce a comprehensive migration guide for users upgrading from v1.9.0 to v1.10.0.

## Context

The workspace contains two versions of the Terraform codebase:
- **v1.9.0**: implemented in `/workspace/terraform-v1.9.0/` (commit 7637a92)
- **v1.10.0**: implemented in `/workspace/terraform-v1.10.0/` (commit 24236f4)

Your task is to identify breaking changes and behavioral changes that affect users, then document migration steps.

## Required Analysis

Your migration guide must cover:

1. **S3 Backend Changes**
   - Removal of deprecated IAM role assumption attributes
   - Migration to `assume_role` block syntax
   - New S3 native state locking support

2. **Moved Blocks Syntax Changes**
   - New requirement to prepend `resource.` identifier when referencing resources with type names matching top-level blocks/keywords
   - Impact on existing `moved` blocks in configurations

3. **Sensitive Value Handling**
   - Changes to mark propagation in conditional expressions
   - When sensitive marks are now preserved (previously lost)
   - How to use `nonsensitive()` to override when needed

4. **Ephemeral Resources and Values** (new feature with migration implications)
   - Introduction of ephemeral input variables and outputs
   - New ephemeral resource mode
   - Impact on secret handling in state files

## Expected Output

Write your migration guide to `/workspace/documentation.md` with the following structure:

1. **Overview** - Summary of the upgrade and major themes
2. **Breaking Changes** - Each breaking change with:
   - What changed and why
   - Before/after code examples
   - Step-by-step migration instructions
   - Common pitfalls to avoid
3. **New Features with Migration Impact** - Features that change how users should write Terraform
4. **Testing Your Migration** - How to validate the upgrade was successful
5. **Rollback Guidance** - How to safely rollback if needed

## Scoring Criteria

Your documentation will be evaluated on:
- **Completeness**: All required breaking changes are documented
- **Accuracy**: Migration steps correctly reflect the actual code changes
- **Clarity**: Instructions are clear with concrete examples
- **Practicality**: Includes testing steps and rollback guidance

## Tips

- Compare the two version directories to identify changes in specific files
- Look for UPGRADE notes in release documentation
- Examine test files to understand behavioral changes
- Check for deprecation warnings and removed features
- Pay attention to changes in internal/backend/remote-state/s3/, internal/terraform/node_resource_abstract.go, and lang/marks/ directories

Good luck!

# Upgrading to Terraform v1.10

-> **Tip:** Use the version selector to view the upgrade guides for older Terraform versions.

Terraform v1.10 is a minor release in the stable Terraform v1.0 series.

Terraform v1.10 honors the
[Terraform v1.0 Compatibility Promises](https://developer.hashicorp.com/terraform/language/v1-compatibility-promises).
There are minor changes that may require additional upgrade steps:

- `moved` blocks will now report an error if `from` or `to` points to a resource type conflicting with reserved keywords

See [the full changelog](https://github.com/hashicorp/terraform/blob/v1.10/CHANGELOG.md)
for more details. If you encounter any problems during upgrading which are not
covered this guide, please start a new topic in
[the Terraform community forum](https://discuss.hashicorp.com/c/terraform-core)
to discuss it.

## Conflicting `moved` block references

Moved blocks now respect reserved keywords such as `local`, `each`, `self` etc. when parsing resource addresses.
Configurations that reference resources with type names that match top level blocks and
keywords from moved blocks will need to prepend the reference identifier with `resource.`.

## S3 Backend 

### S3 Native State Locking

The S3 backend now supports S3 native state locking as an opt-in, experimental feature.
An S3 lock can be used alongside a DynamoDB lock, or independently.
When both locking mechanisms are configured, a lock must be successfully acquired from both locations before subsequent operations will proceed.

To opt-in to S3 native state locking, set `use_lockfile` to `true`.

```terraform
terraform {
  backend "s3" {
    # additional configuration omitted for brevity
    use_lockfile 
... (truncated)

     1→---
     2→page_title: Upgrading to Terraform v1.10
     3→description: Upgrading to Terraform v1.10
     4→---
     5→
     6→# Upgrading to Terraform v1.10
     7→
     8→-> **Tip:** Use the version selector to view the upgrade guides for older Terraform versions.
     9→
    10→Terraform v1.10 is a minor release in the stable Terraform v1.0 series.
    11→
    12→Terraform v1.10 honors the
    13→[Terraform v1.0 Compatibility Promises](https://developer.hashicorp.com/terraform/language/v1-compatibility-promises).
    14→There are minor changes that may require additional upgrade steps:
    15→
    16→- `moved` blocks will now report an error if `from` or `to` points to a resource type conflicting with reserved keywords
    17→
    18→See [the full changelog](https://github.com/hashicorp/terraform/blob/v1.10/CHANGELOG.md)
    19→for more details. If you encounter any problems during upgrading which are not
    20→covered this guide, please start a new topic in
    21→[the Terraform community forum](https://discuss.hashicorp.com/c/terraform-core)
    22→to discuss it.
    23→
    24→## Conflicting `moved` block references
    25→
    26→Moved blocks now respect reserved keywords such as `local`, `each`, `self` etc. when parsing resource addresses.
    27→Configurations that reference resources with type names that match top level blocks and
    28→keywords from moved blocks will need to prepend the reference identifier with `resource.`.
    29→
    30→## S3 Backend 
    31→
    32→### S3 Native State Locking
    33→
    34→The S3 backend now supports S3 native state locking as an opt-in, experimental feature.
    35→An S3 lock can be used alongside a DynamoDB lock, or independently.
    36→When both locking mechanisms are configured, a lock must be successfully 
... (truncated)

     1→---
     2→page_title: moved block configuration reference
     3→description: Learn about the `moved` block that you can specify in Terraform configurations. The `moved` block programmatically changes the location of a resource.
     4→---
     5→
     6→# Moved block configuration reference
     7→
     8→This topic provides reference information for the `moved` block. 
     9→
    10→## Introduction
    11→
    12→The `moved` block programmatically changes the address of a resource. Refer to [Refactoring](/terraform/language/modules/develop/refactoring) for details about how to use the `moved` block in your Terraform configurations.
    13→
    14→## Configuration model
    15→
    16→The following list outlines field hierarchy, language-specific data types, and requirements in the `moved` block. 
    17→
    18→- [`moved`](#moved): map
    19→  - [`from`](#moved): reference
    20→  - [`to`](#moved): reference
    21→
    22→## Complete configuration
    23→
    24→When every field is defined, a `moved` block has the following form:
    25→
    26→```hcl
    27→moved {
    28→    from = <old address for the resource>
    29→    to = <new address for the resource>
    30→}
    31→```
    32→
    33→## Specification
    34→
    35→This section provides details about the fields you can configure in the `moved` block.
    36→
    37→### `moved`
    38→
    39→Map that specifies addresses for the resource. The following table describes the fields you can set in the `moved` block.
    40→
    41→| Field | Description | Type | Required |
    42→| ---   | ---         | ---  | ---      |
    43→| `from` | Specifies a resource's previous address. The syntax allows Terraform to select modules, resources, and resources inside child modules. | string | required |
    44→|

    11→

    13→the module's own source code. This functionality allows you to share modules across different
    14→Terraform configurations, making your module composable and reusable.

    17→set their values using CLI options and environment variables.
    18→When you declare them in [child modules](/terraform/language/modules),
    19→the calling module should pass values in the `module` block.
    20→

    22→compare Terraform modules to function definitions:
    23→
    24→* Input variables are like function arguments.
    25→* [Output values](/terraform/language/values/outputs) are like function return values.
    26→* [Local values](/terraform/language/values/locals) are like a function's temporary local variables.
    27→

    29→"variables" or "Terraform variables" when it is clear from context what sort of
    30→variable is being discussed. Other kinds of variables in Terraform include

    13→
    14→Ephemeral resources are Terraform resources that are essentially temporary. Ephemeral resources have a unique lifecycle, and Terraform does not store them in its state.     15→

    20→specific local name, much like a `resource` block. Terraform uses an ephemeral resource's name to
    21→refer to that resource in the same module, but an ephemeral resource's name has no meaning
    22→outside that module's scope.

    28→

    30→that ephemeral r

     2→page_title: Output Values - Configuration Language
     3→description: Output values are the return values of a Terraform module.

     5→
     6→# Output Values
     7→
     8→Output values make information about your infrastructure available on the
     9→command line, and can expose information for other Terraform configurations to
    10→use. Output values are similar to return values in programming languages.
    11→
    12→> **Hands-on:** Try the [Output Data From
    13→> Terraform](/terraform/tutorials/configuration-language/outputs)
    14→> tutorial.
    15→
    16→Output values have several uses:
    17→
    18→- A child module can use outputs to expose a subset of its resource attributes
    19→  to a parent module.
    20→- A root module can use outputs to print certain values in the CLI output after
    21→  running `terraform apply`.
    22→- When using [remote state](/terraform/language/state/remote), root module outputs can be
    23→  accessed by other configurations via a
    24→  [`terraform_remote_state` data source](/terraform/language/state/remote-state-data).
    25→
    26→Resource instances managed by Terraform each export attributes whose values
    27→can be used elsewhere in configuration. Output values are a way to expose some
    28→of that information to the user of your module.
    29→
    30→-> **Note:** For brevity, output values are often referred to as just "outputs"
    31→when the meaning is clear from context.
    32→
    33→## Declaring an Output Value
    34→
    35→Each output value exported by a module must be declared using an `output`
    36→block:
    37→
    38→```hcl
    39→output "instance_ip_addr" {
    40→  value = aws_instance.server.private_ip
    41→}
    42→```
    43→
    44→The label i

> ## Ephemeral data
> 
> -> **Note**: Ephemeral variables, outputs, and resources are in public beta and available in Terraform v1.10 and later. The workflows of ephemeral values are subject to change.
> 
> Terraform allows you to mark variables and outputs as ephemeral. Providers can also support specific `ephemeral` resources. Ephemerality in Terraform means that the data of a block is available during runtime, but Terraform does not write that data to state or plan files. The `ephemeral` property is helpful when managing credentials, tokens, or other temporary resources you do not want to store in Terraform state files.
> 
> Learn more about defining ephemeral [input variables](/terraform/language/values/variables#exclude-values-from-state), [outputs](/terraform/language/values/outputs#ephemeral-avoid-storing-values-in-state-or-plan-files), and [resources](/terraform/language/resources/ephemeral).

     5→---
     6→
     7→# `ephemeralasnull` function reference
     8→
     9→-> **Note**: The `ephemeralasnull` function is in public beta and available in Terraform v1.10 and later. The workflows of `ephemeralasnull` are subject to change.
    10→
    11→
    12→This topic provides reference information about the `ephemeralasnull` function. The `ephemeralasnull` function accepts an ephemeral value and returns `null`. 
    13→
    14→## Introduction
    15→
    16→You can use the `ephemeralasnull` function to nullify ephemeral values. For example, if you pass an object with a nested ephemeral value to `ephemeralasnull`,it nullifies any ephemeral values within that object.
    17→
    18→## Syntax
    19→
    20→Use the `ephemeralasnull` function with the following syntax:
    21→
    22→```hcl
    23→ephemeralasnull(var.my_ephemeral_value)
    24→```
    25→
    26→In the following example, you can pass the `ephemeralasnull` function an ephemeral value, and the function returns `null`:
    27→
    28→```hcl
    29→variable "example" {
    30→  type      = string
    31→  default   = "test"
    32→  ephemeral = true
    33→}
    34→
    35→# This output returns null.
    36→output "example_output" {
    37→  value       = ephemeralasnull(var.example)
    38→}
    39→```
    40→
    41→For more information on which Terraform blocks can be ephemeral, refer to ephemeral [inputs](/terraform/language/values/variables#exclude-values-from-state), [outputs](/terraform/language/values/outputs#ephemeral-avoid-storing-values-in-state-or-plan-files), and [resources](/

> ## Ephemeral values
> 
> -> **Note**: Ephemeral local values are in public beta and available in Terraform v1.10 and later. The workflows of ephemeral locals are subject to change.
> 
> Local values implicitly become ephemeral if you reference an ephemeral value when you assign that local a value. For example, you can create a local that references an ephemeral `service_token`.
> 
> ```hcl
> variable "service_name" {
>   type    = string
>   default = "forum"
> }
> 
> variable "environment" {
>   type    = string
>   default = "dev"
> }
> 
> variable "service_token" {
>   type      = string
>   ephemeral = true
> }
> 
> locals {
>   service_tag   = "${var.service_name}-${var.environment}"
>   session_token = "${var.service_name}:${var.service_token}"
> }
> ```
> 
> The `local.session_token` value is implicitly ephemeral because it relies on an ephemeral variable.
>

> * [`ephemeral`][inpage-ephemeral] - This variable is available during runtime, but not written to state or plan files.
177a179,220
> ### Exclude values from state
> 
> -> **Note**: Ephemeral variables are in public beta and available in Terraform v1.10 and later. The workflows of ephemeral variables are subject to change.
> 
> [inpage-ephemeral]: #exclude-values-from-state
> 
> Setting a variable as `ephemeral` makes it available during runtime, but Terraform omits ephemeral values from state and plan files. Marking an input variable as `ephemeral` is useful for data that only needs to exist temporarily, such as a short-lived token or session identifier.
> 
> Mark an input variable as ephemeral by setting the `ephemeral` argument to `true`:
> 
> ```hcl
> variable "session_token" {
>   type      = string
>   ephemeral = true
> }
> ```
> 
> Ephemeral variables are available during the current Terraform operation, and Terraform does not store them in state or plan files. So, unlike [`sensitive`](#sensitive) inputs, Terraform ensures ephemeral values are not available beyond the lifetime of the current Terraform run.
> 
> You can only reference ephemeral variables in specific contexts or Terraform throws an error. The following are valid contexts for referencing ephemeral variables:
> * Another ephemeral variable
> * In [local values](/terraform/language/values/locals#ephemeral-values)
> * In [ephemeral resources](/terraform/language/resources/ephemeral)
> * In [ephemeral outputs](/terraform/language/values/outputs#ephemeral-avoid-storing-values-in-state-or-plan-files)
> 
> If another expression references an `ephemeral` variable, that expression implicitly becomes ephemeral.
> 
> ```hcl
> variable "password" {
>   type      = string
>   ephemeral = true
> }
>

< `output` blocks can optionally include `description`, `sensitive`, and `depends_on` arguments, which are described in the following sections.
---
> `output` blocks can optionally include `description`, `sensitive`, `ephemeral`, and `depends_on` arguments, which are described in the following sections.
110a111,140
> <a id="ephemeral"></a>
> 
> ### `ephemeral` — Avoid storing values in state or plan files
> 
> -> **Note**: Ephemeral outputs are in public beta and available in Terraform v1.10 and later. The workflows of ephemeral outputs are subject to change.
> 
> You can mark `output` values as `ephemeral` in child modules to pass ephemeral values between modules while avoiding persisting those values to state or plan files. This is useful for managing credentials, tokens, or other temporary resources you do not want to store in Terraform state files.
> 
> You can mark an `output` in a child module as ephemeral by setting the `ephemeral` attribute to `true`:
> 
> ```hcl
> # modules/db/main.tf
> 
> output "secret_id" {
>   value       = aws_secretsmanager_secret.secret_id
>   description = "Temporary secret ID for accessing database in AWS."
>   ephemeral   = true
> }
> ```
> 
> Terraform has access to the value of `output` blocks during plan and apply operations. At the end of a plan or apply operation, Terraform does not persist the value of any ephemeral outputs.
> 
> You can only reference ephemeral outputs in specific contexts or Terraform throws an error. The following are valid contexts for referencing ephemeral outputs:
> * In another child module's ephemeral `output` block
> * In [ephemeral variables](/terraform/language/values/variables#exclude-values-from-state)
> * In [ephemeral resources](/terraform/language/resources/ephemeral)
> 
> Note that you can

     1→---
     2→page_title: Conditional Expressions - Configuration Language
     3→description: >-
     4→  Conditional expressions select one of two values. You can use them to define
     5→  defaults to replace invalid values.
     6→---
     7→
     8→# Conditional Expressions
     9→
    10→A _conditional expression_ uses the value of a boolean expression to select one of
    11→two values.
    12→
    13→> **Hands-on:** Try the [Create Dynamic Expressions](/terraform/tutorials/configuration-language/expressions?utm_source=WEBSITE&utm_medium=WEB_IO&utm_offer=ARTICLE_PAGE&utm_content=DOCS) tutorial.
    14→
    15→## Syntax
    16→
    17→The syntax of a conditional expression is as follows:
    18→
    19→```hcl
    20→condition ? true_val : false_val
    21→```
    22→
    23→If `condition` is `true` then the result is `true_val`. If `condition` is
    24→`false` then the result is `false_val`.
    25→
    26→A common use of conditional expressions is to define defaults to replace
    27→invalid values:
    28→
    29→```hcl
    30→var.a != "" ? var.a : "default-a"
    31→```
    32→
    33→If `var.a` is an empty string then the result is `"default-a"`, but otherwise
    34→it is the actual value of `var.a`.
    35→
    36→## Conditions
    37→
    38→The condition can be any expression that resolves to a boolean value. This will
    39→usually be an expression that uses the equality, comparison, or logical
    40→operators.
    41→
    42→### Custom Condition Checks
    43→
    44→You can create conditions that produce custom error messages for several types of objects in a configuration. For example, you can add a condition to an input variable that checks whether incoming image IDs are formatted properly.
    45→
    46→Custom conditions can help capture ass
... (truncated)

# Terraform v1.10.0 Changelog Summary

## New Features

**Ephemeral Resources & Values**: The release introduces ephemeral resources that "are read anew during each phase of Terraform evaluation, and cannot be persisted to state storage." Ephemeral input variables and outputs are supported, along with a new `ephemeralasnull` function for converting ephemeral values to null.

## Key Bug Fixes

Notable corrections include:
- Kubernetes backend `secret_suffix` validation improvements
- Resolution of sensitive mark transfer issues causing incorrect plan changes
- Fixed GitHub module source ref handling with unencoded slashes
- Postcondition evaluation errors with multiple resource instances
- Invalid date generation in the `planstimestamp()` function
- S3 backend tag handling preferences

## Breaking Changes (Upgrade Notes)

- S3 backend removes deprecated IAM role assumption attributes; must use `assume_role` block
- S3 backend introduces native state locking; DynamoDB-based locking will eventually deprecate
- `moved` blocks now respect reserved keywords in resource address parsing
- Conditional expressions and for-expressions now consistently propagate marks, potentially showing additional sensitive results

## Enhancements

Performance improvements for resource evaluation with large instance counts, negative index support in the `element` function, and enhanced import block validation during `terraform validate`.
199-assistanttool_useWebFetch
200-usertool_resultWebFetch
# Terraform v1.10.0 Release Summary

## Major Features

**Ephemeral Resources & Values**: Terraform now supports ephemeral resources that are "read anew during each phase of Terraform evaluation, and cannot be persisted to state storage." This includes a new `ephemeralasnull` function for converting ephemeral values to null.

## Critical Bug Fixes

Notable corrections address several state management issues:

- Sensitive marks were "incorrectly transferred to nested resource values, causing erroneous changes during a plan"
- Refresh-only plans with output changes are now properly applicable
- The `plantimestamp()` function validation now returns valid dates
- Conditional expressions and for loops no longer lose marks with certain unknown value combinations

## Backend Updates

**S3 Backend**: The service now supports native S3 state locking alongside existing DynamoDB mechanisms. Deprecated IAM role assumption attributes were removed in favor of the `assume_role` block.

**Kubernetes Backend**: Added validation for `secret_suffix` to prevent errors when suffixes end with numerals.

## Configuration & Syntax Changes

Moved blocks now respect reserved keywords, requiring "resource." prefixes for resources with type names matching top-level block keywords.

## Performance Improvements

Enhanced resource evaluation efficiency, particularly for configurations managing large numbers of resource instances.
201-assistanttool_useWebFetch
202-usertool_resultWebFetch
# Terraform v1.10.0 Release Summary

**Release Date:** November 27, 2024

## Major Features

The release introduces two significant capabilities:

**Ephemeral Resources & Values:** Resources can now be marked as ephemeral, meaning they're "read anew during each phase of Terraform evaluation, and cannot be persisted to state storage." This prevents storing temporary or sensitive data in state files.

**New Function:** The `ephemeralasnull` function enables developers to "replace any ephemeral values with non-ephemeral null values" while preserving other data.

## Key Improvements

Multiple bug fixes address critical issues: sensitive marks being incorrectly transferred to nested resources, invalid date returns from `plantimestamp()`, and GitHub module source references being truncated.

Performance enhancements target "resource evaluation, especially when large numbers of resource instances are involved."

## Notable Changes

The S3 backend now supports native state locking alongside existing DynamoDB support. The `moved` block syntax now requires the `resource.` prefix when referencing resources with reserved keyword type names. Additionally, the `-state` flag is now deprecated across plan, apply, and refresh commands.

220-assistanttool_useRead
221-usertool_resultRead
     1→---
     2→page_title: 'Backend Type: s3'
     3→description: Terraform can store state remotely in S3 and lock that state with DynamoDB.
     4→---
     5→
     6→# S3
     7→
     8→Stores the state as a given key in a given bucket on [Amazon S3](https://aws.amazon.com/s3/).
     9→This backend also supports state locking and consistency checking via [Dynamo DB](https://aws.amazon.com/dynamodb/), which can be enabled by setting the `dynamodb_table` field to an existing DynamoDB table name.
    10→A single DynamoDB table can be used to lock multiple remote state files.
    11→Terraform generates key names that include the values of the `bucket` and `key` variables.
    12→
    13→~> **Warning!** It is highly recommended that you enable
    14→[Bucket Versioning](https://docs.aws.amazon.com/AmazonS3/latest/userguide/manage-versioning-examples.html)
    15→on the S3 bucket to allow for state recovery in the case of accidental deletions and human error.
    16→
    17→## Example Configuration
    18→
    19→```hcl
    20→terraform {
    21→  backend "s3" {
    22→    bucket = "example-bucket"
    23→    key    = "path/to/state"
    24→    region = "us-east-1"
    25→  }
    26→}
    27→```
    28→
    29→This assumes we have a bucket created called `mybucket`. The Terraform state is written to the key `path/to/my/key`.
    30→
    31→Note that for the access credentials we recommend using a [partial configuration](/terraform/language/backend#partial-configuration).
    32→
    33→## State Storage
    34→
    35→The S3 backend stores state data in an S3 object at the path set by the `key` parameter in the S3 bucket indicated by the `bucket` parameter.

   119→## Data Source Configuration
   120→
   121→To make use of the S3 remote state in another configuration,
   122→use the [`terraform_remote_state` data source](/terraform/language/state/remote-state-data).
   123→
   124→```hcl
   125→data "terraform_remote_state" "network" {
   126→  backend = "s3"
   127→  config = {
   128→    bucket = "terraform-state-prod"
   129→    key    = "network/terraform.tfstate"
   130→    region = "us-east-1"
   131→  }
   132→}
   133→```
   134→
   135→The `terraform_remote_state` data source will return all of the root module
   136→outputs defined in the referenced remote state (but not any outputs from
   137→nested modules unless they are explicitly output again in the root). An
   138→example output might look like:
   139→
   140→```
   141→data.terraform_remote_state.network:
   142→  id = 2016-10-29 01:57:59.780010914 +0000 UTC
   143→  addresses.# = 2
   144→  addresses.0 = 52.207.220.222
   145→  addresses.1 = 54.196.78.166
   146→  backend = s3
   147→  config.% = 3
   148→  config.bucket = terraform-state-prod
   149→  config.key = network/terraform.tfstate
   150→  config.region = us-east-1
   151→  elb_address = web-elb-790251200.us-east-1.elb.amazonaws.com
   152→  public_subnet_id = subnet-1e05dd33
   153→```

   306→### S3 State Storage
   307→
   308→The following configuration is required:
   309→
   310→* `bucket` - (Required) Name of the S3 Bucket.
   311→* `key` - (Required) Path to the state file inside the S3 Bucket. When using a non-default [workspace](/terraform/language/state/workspaces), the state path will be `<workspace_key_prefix>/<workspace_name>/<key>` (see also the `workspace_key_prefix` configuration).
   312→
   313→The following configuration is optional:
   314→
   315→* `acl` - (Optional) [Canned ACL](https://docs.aws.amazon.com/AmazonS3/latest/userguide/acl-overview.html#canned-acl) to be applied to the state and lock files.
   316→* `encrypt` - (Optional) Enable [server side encryption](https://docs.aws.amazon.com/AmazonS3/latest/userguide/UsingServerSideEncryption.html) of the state and lock files.
   317→* `endpoint` - (Optional, **Deprecated**) Custom endpoint URL for the AWS S3 API.
   318→  Use `endpoints.s3` instead.
   319→* `force_path_style` - (Optional, **Deprecated**) Enable path-style S3 URLs (`https://<HOST>/<BUCKET>` instead of `https://<BUCKET>.<HOST>`).
   320→* `kms_key_id` - (Optional) Amazon Resource Name (ARN) of a Key Management Service (KMS) Key to use for encrypting the state and lock files. Note that if this value is specified, Terraform will need `kms:Encrypt`, `kms:Decrypt` and `kms:GenerateDataKey` permissions on this KMS key.
   321→* `sse_customer_key` - (Optional) The key to use for encrypting state and lock files with [Server-Side Encryption with Customer-Provided Keys (SSE-C)](https://docs.aws.amazon.com/AmazonS3/latest/userguide/ServerSideEncryptionCustomerKeys.html). This is the base64-encoded value of the key, which

234-assistanttext-
The migration guide has been written to `/workspace/documentation.md`. Here's a summary of what was documented:

## Migration Guide Summary

The guide covers four major areas of change between Terraform v1.9.0 and v1.10.0:

### Breaking Changes

1. **S3 Backend: Root-level IAM Role Assumption Attributes Removed**
   - Eight attributes (`role_arn`, `session_name`, `external_id`, `assume_role_duration_seconds`, `assume_role_policy`, `assume_role_policy_arns`, `assume_role_tags`, `assume_role_transitive_tag_keys`) are permanently removed
   - Must migrate to the `assume_role = { ... }` block syntax
   - Key gotcha: `assume_role_duration_seconds` (integer) becomes `duration` (string like `"1h"`)

2. **`moved` Blocks: Reserved Keyword Conflict**
   - Resources with type names matching keywords (`local`, `each`, `self`, `count`, etc.) must now be referenced with the `resource.` prefix in `moved` blocks
   - Example: `from = local.config` → `from = resource.local.config`

### Behavioral Changes

3. **Sensitive Value Mark Propagation (Bug Fix)**
   - Sensitive marks were previously lost in some conditional expressions and `for` expressions involving unknown values
   - Now consistently propagated — expect new `(sensitive value)` annotations or revealed plan changes after upgrade
   - Use `nonsensitive()` to explicitly unwrap where safe

### New Features with Migration Impact

4. **Ephemeral Resources and Values (Public Beta)**
   - New `ephemeral = true` on variables and outputs — values never written to state/plan files
   - New `ephemeral "type" "name" {}` resource blocks for temporary secrets with open/renew/close lifecycle
   - New `ephemeralasnull()` function
   - New `use_lockfile = true` for S3 native state locking (experimental alternative to DynamoDB)

If you encounter any problems during upgrading which are not\ncovered this guide, please start a new topic in\n[the Terraform community forum](https://discuss.hashicorp.com/c/terraform-core)\nto discuss it.\n\n## Conflicting `moved` block references\n\nMoved blocks now respect reserved keywords such as `local`, `each`, `self` etc. when parsing resource addresses.\nConfigurations that reference resources with type names that match top level blocks and\nkeywords from moved blocks will need to prepend the reference identifier with `resource.`.\n\n## S3 Backend \n\n### S3 Native State Locking\n\nThe S3 backend now supports S3 native state locking as an opt-in, experimental feature.\nAn S3 lock can be used alongside a DynamoDB lock, or independently.\nWhen both locking mechanisms are configured, a lock must be successfully acquired from both locations before subsequent operations will proceed.\n\nTo opt-in to S3 native state locking, set `use_lockfile` to `true`.\n\n```terraform\nterraform {\n  backend \"s3\" {\n    # additional configuration omitted for brevity\n    use_lockfile = true\n  }\n}\n```\n\nWith S3 locking enabled, a lock file will be placed in the same location as the state file.\nThe lock file will be named identically to the state file, but with a `.tflock` extension.\n**S3 bucket policies and IAM policies attached to the calling principal may need to be adjusted to include permissions for the new lock file.**\n\nIn a future minor version of Terraform the experimental label will be removed from the `use_lockfile` attribute and attributes related to DynamoDB based locking will be deprecated.\n\n### Root Assume Role Attribute Removal\n\nSeveral root level attributes related to IAM role assumption which were previously deprecated have been removed.\nEach removed field has an analogous field inside the [`assume_role` block](https://developer.hashicorp.com/terraform/language/backend/s3#assume-role-configuration) which should be used instead.\n\n| Removed | Replacement |\n| --- | --- |\n| `role_arn` | `assume_role.role_arn` |\n| `session_name` | `assume_role.session_name` |\n| `external_id` | `assume_role.external_id` |\n| `assume_role_duration_seconds` | `assume_role.duration` |\n| `assume_role_policy` | `assume_role.policy` |\n| `assume_role_policy_arns` | `assume_role.policy_arn` |\n| `assume_role_tags` | `assume_role.tags` |\n| `assume_role_transitive_tag_keys` | `assume_role.transitive_tag_keys` |"

The `moved` block programmatically changes the location of a resource.\n---\n\n# Moved block configuration reference\n\nThis topic provides reference information for the `moved` block. \n\n## Introduction\n\nThe `moved` block programmatically changes the address of a resource. Refer to [Refactoring](/terraform/language/modules/develop/refactoring) for details about how to use the `moved` block in your Terraform configurations.\n\n## Configuration model\n\nThe following list outlines field hierarchy, language-specific data types, and requirements in the `moved` block. \n\n- [`moved`](#moved): map\n  - [`from`](#moved): reference\n  - [`to`](#moved): reference\n\n## Complete configuration\n\nWhen every field is defined, a `moved` block has the following form:\n\n```hcl\nmoved {\n    from = <old address for the resource>\n    to = <new address for the resource>\n}\n```\n\n## Specification\n\nThis section provides details about the fields you can configure in the `moved` block.\n\n### `moved`\n\nMap that specifies addresses for the resource. The following table describes the fields you can set in the `moved` block.\n\n| Field | Description | Type | Required |\n| ---   | ---         | ---  | ---      |\n| `from` | Specifies a resource's previous address. The syntax allows Terraform to select modules, resources, and resources inside child modules. | string | required |\n| `to` | Specifies the new address to relocate the resource to. The syntax allows Terraform to select modules, resources, and resources inside child modules. | string | required |\n\nBefore creating a new plan for the resource specified in the `to` field, Terraform checks the state for an existing object at the address specified in the `from` field. Terraform renames existing objects to the string specified in the `to` field and then creates a plan. The plan directs Terraform to provision the resource specified in the `from` field as the resource specified in the `to` field. As a result, Terraform does not destroy the resource during the Terraform run.\n\n## Example\n\nThe following example moves an AWS instance from address `aws_instance.a` to `aws_instance.b`:\n\n```hcl\nmoved {\n  from = aws_instance.a\n  to   = aws_instance.b\n}\n```\n\n\n\n\n\n",

Output
{
  "file": {
    "content": "---\npage_title: Input Variables - Configuration Language\ndescription: >-\n  Input variables allow you to customize modules without altering their source\n  code. Learn how to declare, define, and reference variables in configurations.\n---\n\n# Input Variables\n\n> **Hands-on:** Try the [Customize Terraform Configuration with Variables](/terraform/tutorials/configuration-language/variables?utm_source=WEBSITE&utm_medium=WEB_IO&utm_offer=ARTICLE_PAGE&utm_content=DOCS) tutorial.\n\nInput variables let you customize aspects of Terraform modules without altering\nthe module's own source code. This functionality allows you to share modules across different\nTerraform configurations, making your module composable and reusable.\n\nWhen you declare variables in the root module of your configuration, you can\nset their values using CLI options and environment variables.\nWhen you declare them in [child modules](/terraform/language/modules),\nthe calling module should pass values in the `module` block.\n\nIf you're familiar with traditional programming languages, it can be useful to\ncompare Terraform modules to function definitions:\n\n* Input variables are like function arguments.\n* [Output values](/terraform/language/values/outputs) are like function return values.\n* [Local values](/terraform/language/values/locals) are like a function's temporary local variables.\n\n-> **Note:** For brevity, input variables are often referred to as just\n\"variables\" or \"Terraform variables\" when it is clear from context what sort of\nvariable is being discussed. Other kinds of variables in Terraform include\n_environment variables_ (set by the shell where Terraform runs) and _expression\nvariables_ (used to indirectly represent a value in an\n[expression](/terraform/language/expressions)).\n\n## Declaring an Input Variable\n\nEach input variable accepted by a module must be declared using a `variable`\nblock:\n\n```hcl\nvariable \"image_id\" {\n  type = string\n}\n\nvariable \"availability_zone_names\" {\n  type    = list(string)\n  default = [\"us-west-1a\"]\n}\n\nvariable \"docker_ports\" {\n  type = list(object({\n    internal = number\n    external = number\n    protocol = string\n  }))\n  default = [\n    {\n      internal = 8300\n      external = 8300\n      protocol = \"tcp\"\n    }\n  ]\n}\n```\n\nThe label after the `variable` keyword is a name for the variable, which must\nbe unique among all variables in the same module. This name is used to\nassign a value to the variable from outside and to reference the variable's\nvalue from within the module.\n\nThe name of a variable can be any valid [identifier](/terraform/language/syntax/configuration#identifiers)\n_except_ the following: `source`, `version`, `providers`, `count`, `for_each`, `lifecycle`, `depends_on`, `locals`.\n\nThese names are reserved for meta-arguments in\n[module configuration blocks](/terraform/language/modules/syntax), and cannot be\ndeclared as variable names.\n\n## Arguments\n\nTerraform CLI defines the following optional arguments for variable declarations:\n\n* [`default`][inpage-default] - A default value which then makes the variable optional.\n* [`type`][inpage-type] - This argument specifies what value types are accepted for the variable.\n* [`description`][inpage-description] - This specifies the input variable's documentation.\n* [`validation`][inpage-validation] - A block to define validation rules, usually in addition to type constraints.\n* [`ephemeral`][inpage-ephemeral] - This variable is available during runtime, but not written to state or plan files.\n* [`sensitive`][inpage-sensitive] - Limits Terraform UI output when the variable is used in configuration.\n* [`nullable`][inpage-nullable] - Specify if the variable can be `null` within the module.\n\n### Default values\n\n[inpage-default]: #default-values\n\nThe variable declaration can also include a `default` argument. If present,\nthe variable is considered to be _optional_ and the default value will be used\nif no value is set when calling the module or running Terraform. The `default`\nargument requires a literal value and cannot reference other objects in the\nconfiguration.\n\n### Type Constraints\n\n[inpage-type]: #type-constraints\n\nThe `type` argument in a `variable` block allows you to restrict the\n[type of value](/terraform/language/expressions/types) that will be accepted as\nthe value for a variable. If no type constraint is set then a value of any type\nis accepted.\n\nWhile type constraints are optional, we recommend specifying them; they\ncan serve as helpful reminders for users of the module, and they\nallow Terraform to return a helpful error message if the wrong type is used.\n\nType constraints are created from a mixture of type keywords and type\nconstructors. The supported type keywords are:\n\n* `string`\n* `number`\n* `bool`\n\nThe type constructors allow you to specify complex types such as\ncollections:\n\n* `list(<TYPE>)`\n* `set(<TYPE>)`\n* `map(<TYPE>)`\n* `object({<ATTR NAME> = <TYPE>, ... })`\n* `tuple([<TYPE>, ...])`\n\nThe keyword `any` may be used to indicate that any type is acceptable. For\nmore information on the meaning and behavior of these different types, as well\nas detailed information about automatic conversion of complex types, see\n[Type Constraints](/terraform/language/expressions/types).\n\nIf both the `type` and `default` arguments are specified, the given default\nvalue must be convertible to the specified type.\n\n### Input Variable Documentation\n\n[inpage-description]: #input-variable-documentation\n\nBecause the input variables of a module are part of its user interface, you can\nbriefly describe the purpose of each variable using the optional\n`description` argument:\n\n```hcl\nvariable \"image_id\" {\n  type        = string\n  description = \"The id of the machine image (AMI) to use for the server.\"\n}\n```\n\nThe description should concisely explain the purpose\nof the variable and what kind of value is expected. This description string\nmight be included in documentation about the module, and so it should be written\nfrom the perspective of the user of the module rather than its maintainer. For\ncommentary for module maintainers, use comments.\n\n### Custom Validation Rules\n\n[inpage-validation]: #custom-validation-rules\n\n-> This feature was introduced in Terraform CLI v0.13.0.\n\nYou can specify custom validation rules for a particular variable by adding a `validation` block within the corresponding `variable` block. The example below checks whether the AMI ID has the correct syntax.\n\n```hcl\nvariable \"image_id\" {\n  type        = string\n  description = \"The id of the machine image (AMI) to use for the server.\"\n\n  validation {\n    condition     = length(var.image_id) > 4 && substr(var.image_id, 0, 4) == \"ami-\"\n    error_message = \"The image_id value must be a valid AMI id, starting with \\\"ami-\\\".\"\n  }\n}\n```\nRefer to [Custom Condition Checks](/terraform/language/expressions/custom-conditions#input-variable-validation) for more details.\n\n### Exclude values from state\n\n-> **Note**: Ephemeral variables are in public beta and available in Terraform v1.10 and later. The workflows of ephemeral variables are subject to change.\n\n[inpage-ephemeral]: #exclude-values-from-state\n\nSetting a variable as `ephemeral` makes it available during runtime, but Terraform omits ephemeral values from state and plan files. Marking an input variable as `ephemeral` is useful for data that only needs to exist temporarily, such as a short-lived token or session identifier.\n\nMark an input variable as ephemeral by setting the `ephemeral` argument to `true`:\n\n```hcl\nvariable \"session_token\" {\n  type      = string\n  ephemeral = true\n}\n```\n\nEphemeral variables are available during the current Terraform operation, and Terraform does not store them in state or plan files. So, unlike [`sensitive`](#sensitive) inputs, Terraform ensures ephemeral values are not available beyond the lifetime of the current Terraform run.\n\nYou can only reference ephemeral variables in specific contexts or Terraform throws an error. The following are valid contexts for referencing ephemeral variables:\n* Another ephemeral variable\n* In [local values](/terraform/language/values/locals#ephemeral-values)\n* In [ephemeral resources](/terraform/language/resources/ephemeral)\n* In [ephemeral outputs](/terraform/language/values/outputs#ephemeral-avoid-storing-values-in-state-or-plan-files)\n\nIf another expression references an `ephemeral` variable, that expression implicitly becomes ephemeral.\n\n```hcl\nvariable \"password\" {\n  type      = string\n  ephemeral = true\n}\n\nlocals {\n  # local.database_password is implicitly ephemeral because \n  # var.password is ephemeral.\n  database_password = var.password\n}\n```\n\nThe `local.database_password` value is implicitly ephemeral because it depends on `var.password`.\n\n### Suppressing Values in CLI Output\n\n[inpage-sensitive]: #suppressing-values-in-cli-output\n\n> **Hands-on:** Try the [Protect Sensitive Input Variables](/terraform/tutorials/configuration-language/sensitive-variables?utm_source=WEBSITE&utm_medium=WEB_IO&utm_offer=ARTICLE_PAGE&utm_content=DOCS) tutorial.\n\nSetting a variable as `sensitive` prevents Terraform from showing its value in\nthe `plan` or `apply` output, when you use that variable elsewhere in your\nconfiguration.\n\nTerraform will still record sensitive values in the [state](/terraform/language/state),\nand so anyone who can access the state data will have access to the sensitive\nvalues in cleartext. If you want to omit a value from state, mark that value as [`ephemeral`](#ephemeral). For more information, refer to\n[Sensitive Data in State](/terraform/language/state/sensitive-data).\n\nDeclare a variable as sensitive by setting the `sensitive` argument to `true`:\n\n```hcl\nvariable \"user_information\" {\n  type = object({\n    name    = string\n    address = string\n  })\n  sensitive = true\n}\n\nresource \"some_resource\" \"a\" {\n  name    = var.user_information.name\n  address = var.user_information.address\n}\n```\n\nAny expressions whose result depends on the sensitive variable will be treated\nas sensitive themselves, and so in the above example the two arguments of\n`resource \"some_resource\" \"a\"` will also be hidden in the plan output:\n\n```\nTerraform will perform the following actions:\n\n  # some_resource.a will be created\n  + resource \"some_resource\" \"a\" {\n      + name    = (sensitive value)\n      + address = (sensitive value)\n    }\n\nPlan: 1 to add, 0 to change, 0 to destroy.\n```\n\nIn some cases where you use a sensitive variable inside a nested block, Terraform\nmay treat the entire block as redacted. This happens for resource types where\nall of the blocks of a particular type are required to be unique, and so\ndisclosing the content of one block might imply the content of a sibling block.\n\n```\n  # some_resource.a will be updated in-place\n  ~ resource \"some_resource\" \"a\" {\n      ~ nested_block {\n          # At least one attribute in this block is (or was) sensitive,\n          # so its contents will not be displayed.\n        }\n    }\n```\n\nA provider can also\n[declare an attribute as sensitive](/terraform/plugin/sdkv2/best-practices/sensitive-state#using-the-sensitive-flag),\nwhich will cause Terraform to hide it from regular output regardless of how\nyou assign it a value. For more information, see\n[Sensitive Resource Attributes](/terraform/language/expressions/references#sensitive-resource-attributes).\n\nIf you use a sensitive value as part of an\n[output value](/terraform/language/values/outputs) then Terraform will require\nyou to also mark the output value itself as sensitive, to confirm that you\nintended to export it.\n\n#### Cases where Terraform may disclose a sensitive variable\n\nA `sensitive` variable is a configuration-centered concept, and values are sent to providers without any obfuscation. A provider error could disclose a value if that value is included in the error message. For example, a provider might return the following error even if \"foo\" is a sensitive value: `\"Invalid value 'foo' for field\"`\n\nIf a resource attribute is used as, or part of, the provider-defined resource id, an `apply` will disclose the value. In the example below, the `prefix` attribute has been set to a sensitive variable, but then that value (\"jae\") is later disclosed as part of the resource id:\n\n```\n  # random_pet.animal will be created\n  + resource \"random_pet\" \"animal\" {\n      + id        = (known after apply)\n      + length    = 2\n      + prefix    = (sensitive value)\n      + separator = \"-\"\n    }\n\nPlan: 1 to add, 0 to change, 0 to destroy.\n\n...\n\nrandom_pet.animal: Creating...\nrandom_pet.animal: Creation complete after 0s [id=jae-known-mongoose]\n```\n\n### Disallowing Null Input Values\n\n[inpage-nullable]: #disallowing-null-input-values\n\n-> This feature is available in Terraform v1.1.0 and later.\n\nThe `nullable` argument in a variable block controls whether the module caller\nmay assign the value `null` to the variable.\n\n```hcl\nvariable \"example\" {\n  type     = string\n  nullable = false\n}\n```\n\nThe default value for `nullable` is `true`. When `nullable` is `true`, `null`\nis a valid value for the variable, and the module configuration must always\naccount for the possibility of the variable value being `null`. Passing a\n`null` value as a module input argument will override any `default` value.\n\nSetting `nullable` to `false` ensures that the variable value will never be\n`null` within the module. If `nullable` is `false` and the variable has a\n`default` value, then Terraform uses the default when a module input argument is `null`.\n\nThe `nullable` argument only controls where the direct value of the variable may be `null`.\nFor variables of collection or structural types, such as lists or objects,\nthe caller may still use `null` in nested elements or attributes, as long as\nthe collection or structure itself is not null.\n\n## Using Input Variable Values\n\nWithin the module that declared a variable, its value can be accessed from\nwithin [expressions](/terraform/language/expressions) as `var.<NAME>`,\nwhere `<NAME>` matches the label given in the declaration block:\n\n-> **Note:** Input variables are _created_ by a `variable` block, but you\n_reference_ them as attributes on an object named `var`.\n\n```hcl\nresource \"aws_instance\" \"example\" {\n  instance_type = \"t2.micro\"\n  ami           = var.image_id\n}\n```\n\nThe value assigned to a variable can only be accessed in expressions within\nthe module where it was declared.\n\n## Assigning Values to Root Module Variables\n\nWhen variables are declared in the root module of your configuration, they\ncan be set in a number of ways:\n\n* [In an HCP Terraform workspace](/terraform/cloud-docs/workspaces/variables).\n* Individually, with the `-var` command line option.\n* In variable definitions (`.tfvars`) files, either specified on the command line\n  or automatically loaded.\n* As environment variables.\n\nThe following sections describe these options in more detail. This section does\nnot apply to _child_ modules, where values for input variables are instead\nassigned in the configuration of their parent module, as described in\n[_Modules_](/terraform/language/modules).\n\n### Variables on the Command Line\n\nTo specify individual variables on the command line, use the `-var` option\nwhen running the `terraform plan` and `terraform apply` commands:\n\n```\nterraform apply -var=\"image_id=ami-abc123\"\nterraform apply -var='image_id_list=[\"ami-abc123\",\"ami-def456\"]' -var=\"instance_type=t2.micro\"\nterraform apply -var='image_id_map={\"us-east-1\":\"ami-abc123\",\"us-east-2\":\"ami-def456\"}'\n```\n\nThe above examples show appropriate syntax for Unix-style shells, such as on\nLinux or macOS. For more information on shell quoting, including additional\nexamples for Windows Command Prompt, see\n[Input Variables on the Command Line](/terraform/cli/commands/plan#input-variables-on-the-command-line).\n\nYou can use the `-var` option multiple times in a single command to set several\ndifferent variables.\n\n<a id=\"variable-files\"></a>\n\n### Variable Definitions (`.tfvars`) Files\n\nTo set lots of variables, it is more convenient to specify their values in\na _variable definitions file_ (with a filename ending in either `.tfvars`\nor `.tfvars.json`) and then specify that file on the command line with\n`-var-file`:\n\nLinux, Mac OS, and UNIX:\n\n```shell\nterraform apply -var-file=\"testing.tfvars\"\n```\n\nPowerShell:\n\n```shell\nterraform apply -var-file='testing.tfvars'\n```\n\nWindows `cmd.exe`:\n\n```shell\nterraform apply -var-file=\"testing.tfvars\"\n```\n\n-> **Note:** This is how HCP Terraform passes\n[workspace variables](/terraform/cloud-docs/workspaces/variables) to Terraform.\n\nA variable definitions file uses the same basic syntax as Terraform language\nfiles, but consists only of variable name assignments:\n\n```hcl\nimage_id = \"ami-abc123\"\navailability_zone_names = [\n  \"us-east-1a\",\n  \"us-west-1c\",\n]\n```\n\nTerraform also automatically loads a number of variable definitions files\nif they are present:\n\n* Files named exactly `terraform.tfvars` or `terraform.tfvars.json`.\n* Any files with names ending in `.auto.tfvars` or `.auto.tfvars.json`.\n\nFiles whose names end with `.json` are parsed instead as JSON objects, with\nthe root object properties corresponding to variable names:\n\n```json\n{\n  \"image_id\": \"ami-abc123\",\n  \"availability_zone_names\": [\"us-west-1a\", \"us-west-1c\"]\n}\n```\n\n### Environment Variables\n\nAs a fallback for the other ways of defining variables, Terraform searches\nthe environment of its own process for environment variables named `TF_VAR_`\nfollowed by the name of a declared variable.\n\nThis can be useful when running Terraform in automation, or when running a\nsequence of Terraform commands in succession with the same variables.\nFor example, at a `bash` prompt on a Unix system:\n\n```\n$ export TF_VAR_image_id=ami-abc123\n$ terraform plan\n...\n```\n\nOn operating systems where environment variable names are case-sensitive,\nTerraform matches the variable name exactly as given in configuration, and\nso the required environment variable name will usually have a mix of upper\nand lower case letters as in the above example.\n\n### Complex-typed Values\n\nWhen variable values are provided in a variable definitions file, you can use\nTerraform's usual syntax for\n[literal expressions](/terraform/language/expressions/types#literal-expressions)\nto assign complex-typed values, like lists and maps.\n\nSome special rules apply to the `-var` command line option and to environment\nvariables. For convenience, Terraform defaults to interpreting `-var` and\nenvironment variable values as literal strings, which need only shell quoting,\nand no special quoting for Terraform. For example, in a Unix-style shell:\n\n```\n$ export TF_VAR_image_id='ami-abc123'\n```\n\nHowever, if a root module variable uses a [type constraint](#type-constraints)\nto require a complex value (list, set, map, object, or tuple), Terraform will\ninstead attempt to parse its value using the same syntax used within variable\ndefinitions files, which requires careful attention to the string escaping rules\nin your shell:\n\n```\n$ export TF_VAR_availability_zone_names='[\"us-west-1b\",\"us-west-1d\"]'\n```\n\nFor readability, and to avoid the need to worry about shell escaping, we\nrecommend always setting complex variable values via variable definitions files.\nFor more information on quoting and escaping for `-var` arguments,\nsee\n[Input Variables on the Command Line](/terraform/cli/commands/plan#input-variables-on-the-command-line).\n\n### Values for Undeclared Variables\n\nIf you have defined a variable value, but not its corresponding `variable {}`\ndefinition, you may get an error or warning depending on how you have provided\nthat value.\n\nIf you provide values for undeclared variables defined as [environment variables](#environment-variables)\nyou will not get an error or warning. This is because environment variables may\nbe declared but not used in all configurations that might be run.\n\nIf you provide values for undeclared variables defined [in a file](#variable-definitions-tfvars-files)\nyou will get a warning. This is to help in cases where you have provided a variable\nvalue _meant_ for a variable declaration, but perhaps there is a mistake in the\nvalue definition. For example, the following configuration:\n\n```terraform\nvariable \"moose\" {\n  type = string\n}\n```\n\nAnd the following `.tfvars` file:\n\n```hcl\nmosse = \"Moose\"\n```\n\nWill cause Terraform to warn you that there is no variable declared `\"mosse\"`, which can help\nyou spot this mistake.\n\nIf you use `.tfvars` files across multiple configurations and expect to continue to see this warning,\nyou can use the [`-compact-warnings`](/terraform/cli/commands/plan#compact-warnings)\noption to simplify your output.\n\nIf you provide values for undeclared variables on the [command line](#variables-on-the-command-line),\nTerraform will return an error. To avoid this error, either declare a variable block for the value, or remove\nthe variable value from your Terraform call.\n\n### Variable Definition Precedence\n\nThe above mechanisms for setting variables can be used together in any\ncombination. If the same variable is assigned multiple values, Terraform uses\nthe _last_ value it finds, overriding any previous values. Note that the same\nvariable cannot be assigned multiple values within a single source.\n\nTerraform loads variables in the following order, with later sources taking\nprecedence over earlier ones:\n\n* Environment variables\n* The `terraform.tfvars` file, if present.\n* The `terraform.tfvars.json` file, if present.\n* Any `*.auto.tfvars` or `*.auto.tfvars.json` files, processed in lexical order\n  of their filenames.\n* Any `-var` and `-var-file` options on the command line, in the order they\n  are provided. (This includes variables set by an HCP Terraform\n  workspace.)\n\n~> **Important:** In Terraform 0.12 and later, variables with map and object\nvalues behave the same way as other variables: the last value found overrides\nthe previous values. This is a change from previous versions of Terraform, which\nwould _merge_ map values instead of overriding them.\n\n#### Variable precedence within Terraform tests\n\nWithin Terraform test files, you can specify variable values within\n`variables` blocks, either nested within `run` blocks or defined directly within\nthe file.\n\nVariables defined in this way take precedence over all other mechanisms during\ntest execution, with variables defined within `run` blocks taking precedence\nover those defined within the file.\n",

Ephemeral resource blocks represent temporary resources that Terraform does not store in state.\n---\n\n# Ephemeral resource configuration reference\n\nThis topic provides reference information for the `ephemeral` block. \n\n-> **Note**: Ephemeral resources are in public beta and available in Terraform v1.10 and later. The workflows of ephemeral resources are subject to change.\n\n## Introduction\n\nEphemeral resources are Terraform resources that are essentially temporary. Ephemeral resources have a unique lifecycle, and Terraform does not store them in its state. Each `ephemeral` block describes one or more ephemeral resources, such as a temporary password or connection to another system.\n\n<!-- After page exists, add: For information about how Terraform manages ephemeral resources, refer to\n[Epehemeral Values](/terraform/language/epehemeral-values). -->\n\nAn `ephemeral` block declares an ephemeral resource of a specific type with a\nspecific local name, much like a `resource` block. Terraform uses an ephemeral resource's name to\nrefer to that resource in the same module, but an ephemeral resource's name has no meaning\noutside that module's scope.\n\n\n## Lifecycle\n\nThe lifecycle of an ephemeral resource is different from resources and data sources. When Terraform provisions ephemeral resources, it performs the following steps:\n\n1. If Terraform needs to access the result of an ephemeral resource, it opens\nthat ephemeral resource. For example, if Terraform opens an ephemeral resource for a Vault secret, the Vault provider obtains a lease and returns a secret. \n\n1. If Terraform needs access to the ephemeral resource for longer than the\nremote system's enforced expiration time, Terraform asks the provider\nto renew it periodically. For example, if Terraform renews a Vault secret ephemeral resource, the Vault provider then calls Vault's lease renewal API endpoint to extend the expiration time.\n\n1. Once Terraform no longer needs an ephemeral resource, Terraform closes\nit. This happens after the providers that depend on a certain ephemeral resource\ncomplete all of their work for the current Terraform run phase. For example, closing a Vault secret ephemeral resource means the Vault provider explicitly ends the lease, allowing Vault to immediately revoke the associated credentials.\n\nTerraform follows these lifecycle steps for each instance of an ephemeral\nresource in a given configuration.\n\n## Dependency graph\n\nEphemeral resources form nodes in Terraform's dependency graph, which interact similarly as resources and data sources. For example, when a resource or data source depends on an attribute of an ephemeral resource, Terraform automatically provisions the ephemeral resource first.\n\n## Configuration model\n\nMost of the arguments within the body of an `ephemeral` block are specific to the ephemeral resource you are defining. As with resources and data sources, each provider in the [Terraform Registry](https://registry.terraform.io/browse/providers) includes documentation for the ephemeral resources it supports, if any. An ephemeral resource type's documentation lists which arguments are available and how you should format your resource's values. \n\nThe following list outlines general field hierarchy, language-specific data types, and requirements in the `ephemeral` block. \n\n- [`ephemeral`](#ephemeral): map\n  - [`attributes`](#ephemeral)\n  - [`meta-arguments`](#ephemeral)\n\n## Complete configuration\n\nAn `ephemeral` block has the following form:\n\n```hcl\nephemeral \"<resource_type>\" \"<resource_name>\" {\n  <attributes>\n  <meta-arguments>\n}\n```\n\n## Referencing ephemeral resources\n\nYou can only reference ephemeral resources in specific ephemeral contexts or\nTerraform throws an error. The following are valid contexts for referencing\nephemeral resources:\n\n* In another ephemeral resource\n* In [local values](/terraform/language/values/locals#ephemeral-values)\n* In [ephemeral variables](/terraform/language/values/variables#exclude-values-from-state)\n* In [ephemeral outputs](/terraform/language/values/outputs#ephemeral-avoid-storing-values-in-state-or-plan-files)\n* Configuring providers in the `provider` block\n* In [provisioner](/terraform/language/resources/provisioners/syntax) and [connection](/terraform/language/resources/provisioners/connection) blocks\n\n## Meta-arguments\n\nYou can use the following meta-arguments with ephemeral resources to change the behavior of those resources. The following meta-arguments work the same way for resources, data sources, and ephemeral\nresources:\n\n- [`depends_on`, for specifying hidden dependencies](/terraform/language/meta-arguments/depends_on)\n- [`count`, for creating multiple resource instances according to a count](/terraform/language/meta-arguments/count)\n- [`for_each`, to create multiple instances according to a map or set of strings](/terraform/language/meta-arguments/for_each)\n- [`provider`, for selecting a non-default provider configuration](/terraform/language/meta-arguments/resource-provider)\n- [`lifecycle`, for lifecycle customizations](/terraform/language/meta-arguments/lifecycle)\n\nEphemeral resources do not support the `provisioner` meta-argument.\n\n## Example\n\nThe following example configures the `postgresql` provider with credentials from\nan ephemeral resource. Since these credentials are managed by an ephemeral resource, Terraform does not store them in your state or plan files.\n\n```hcl\nephemeral \"aws_secretsmanager_secret_version\" \"db_master\" {\n  secret_id = data.aws_db_instance.example.master_user_secret[0].secret_arn\n}\n\nlocals {\n  credentials = jsondecode(ephemeral.aws_secretsmanager_secret_version.db_master.secret_string)\n}\n\nprovider \"postgresql\" {\n  host     = data.aws_db_instance.example.address\n  port     = data.aws_db_instance.example.port\n  username = local.credentials[\"username\"]\n  password = local.credentials[\"password\"]\n}\n```\n",

    "content": "---\npage_title: Output Values - Configuration Language\ndescription: Output values are the return values of a Terraform module.\n---\n\n# Output Values\n\nOutput values make information about your infrastructure available on the\ncommand line, and can expose information for other Terraform configurations to\nuse. Output values are similar to return values in programming languages.\n\n> **Hands-on:** Try the [Output Data From\n> Terraform](/terraform/tutorials/configuration-language/outputs)\n> tutorial.\n\nOutput values have several uses:\n\n- A child module can use outputs to expose a subset of its resource attributes\n  to a parent module.\n- A root module can use outputs to print certain values in the CLI output after\n  running `terraform apply`.\n- When using [remote state](/terraform/language/state/remote), root module outputs can be\n  accessed by other configurations via a\n  [`terraform_remote_state` data source](/terraform/language/state/remote-state-data).\n\nResource instances managed by Terraform each export attributes whose values\ncan be used elsewhere in configuration. Output values are a way to expose some\nof that information to the user of your module.\n\n-> **Note:** For brevity, output values are often referred to as just \"outputs\"\nwhen the meaning is clear from context.\n\n## Declaring an Output Value\n\nEach output value exported by a module must be declared using an `output`\nblock:\n\n```hcl\noutput \"instance_ip_addr\" {\n  value = aws_instance.server.private_ip\n}\n```\n\nThe label immediately after the `output` keyword is the name, which must be a\nvalid [identifier](/terraform/language/syntax/configuration#identifiers). In a root module, this name is\ndisplayed to the user; in a child module, it can be used to access the output's\nvalue.\n\nThe `value` argument takes an [expression](/terraform/language/expressions)\nwhose result is to be returned to the user. In this example, the expression\nrefers to the `private_ip` attribute exposed by an `aws_instance` resource\ndefined elsewhere in this module (not shown). Any valid expression is allowed\nas an output value.\n\n-> **Note:** Outputs are only rendered when Terraform applies your plan. Running\n`terraform plan` will not render outputs.\n\n## Accessing Child Module Outputs\n\nIn a parent module, outputs of child modules are available in expressions as\n`module.<MODULE NAME>.<OUTPUT NAME>`. For example, if a child module named\n`web_server` declared an output named `instance_ip_addr`, you could access that\nvalue as `module.web_server.instance_ip_addr`.\n\n\n## Custom Condition Checks\n\nYou can use `precondition` blocks to specify guarantees about output data. The following examples creates a precondition that checks whether the EC2 instance has an encrypted root volume.\n\n```hcl\noutput \"api_base_url\" {\n  value = \"https://${aws_instance.example.private_dns}:8433/\"\n\n  # The EC2 instance must have an encrypted root volume.\n  precondition {\n    condition     = data.aws_ebs_volume.example.encrypted\n    error_message = \"The server's root volume is not encrypted.\"\n  }\n}\n```\n\nCustom conditions can help capture assumptions, helping future maintainers understand the configuration design and intent. They also return useful information about errors earlier and in context, helping consumers more easily diagnose issues in their configurations.\n\nRefer to [Custom Condition Checks](/terraform/language/expressions/custom-conditions#preconditions-and-postconditions) for more details.\n\n## Optional Arguments\n\n`output` blocks can optionally include `description`, `sensitive`, `ephemeral`, and `depends_on` arguments, which are described in the following sections.\n\n<a id=\"description\"></a>\n\n### `description` \u2014 Output Value Documentation\n\nBecause the output values of a module are part of its user interface, you can\nbriefly describe the purpose of each value using the optional `description`\nargument:\n\n```hcl\noutput \"instance_ip_addr\" {\n  value       = aws_instance.server.private_ip\n  description = \"The private IP address of the main server instance.\"\n}\n```\n\nThe description should concisely explain the\npurpose of the output and what kind of value is expected. This description\nstring might be included in documentation about the module, and so it should be\nwritten from the perspective of the user of the module rather than its\nmaintainer. For commentary for module maintainers, use comments.\n\n<a id=\"ephemeral\"></a>\n\n### `ephemeral` \u2014 Avoid storing values in state or plan files\n\n-> **Note**: Ephemeral outputs are in public beta and available in Terraform v1.10 and later. The workflows of ephemeral outputs are subject to change.\n\nYou can mark `output` values as `ephemeral` in child modules to pass ephemeral values between modules while avoiding persisting those values to state or plan files. This is useful for managing credentials, tokens, or other temporary resources you do not want to store in Terraform state files.\n\nYou can mark an `output` in a child module as ephemeral by setting the `ephemeral` attribute to `true`:\n\n```hcl\n# modules/db/main.tf\n\noutput \"secret_id\" {\n  value       = aws_secretsmanager_secret.secret_id\n  description = \"Temporary secret ID for accessing database in AWS.\"\n  ephemeral   = true\n}\n```\n\nTerraform has access to the value of `output` blocks during plan and apply operations. At the end of a plan or apply operation, Terraform does not persist the value of any ephemeral outputs.\n\nYou can only reference ephemeral outputs in specific contexts or Terraform throws an error. The following are valid contexts for referencing ephemeral outputs:\n* In another child module's ephemeral `output` block\n* In [ephemeral variables](/terraform/language/values/variables#exclude-values-from-state)\n* In [ephemeral resources](/terraform/language/resources/ephemeral)\n\nNote that you cannot set an `output` value as `ephemeral` in the root module.\n\n\n<a id=\"sensitive\"></a>\n\n### `sensitive` \u2014 Suppressing Values in CLI Output\n\nAn output can be marked as containing sensitive material using the optional\n`sensitive` argument:\n\n```hcl\noutput \"db_password\" {\n  value       = aws_db_instance.db.password\n  description = \"The password for logging in to the database.\"\n  sensitive   = true\n}\n```\n\nTerraform will hide values marked as sensitive in the messages from\n`terraform plan` and `terraform apply`. In the following scenario, our root\nmodule has an output declared as sensitive and a module call with a\nsensitive output, which we then use in a resource attribute.\n\n```hcl\n# main.tf\n\nmodule \"foo\" {\n  source = \"./mod\"\n}\n\nresource \"test_instance\" \"x\" {\n  some_attribute = module.foo.a # resource attribute references a sensitive output\n}\n\noutput \"out\" {\n  value     = \"xyz\"\n  sensitive = true\n}\n\n# mod/main.tf, our module containing a sensitive output\n\noutput \"a\" {\n  value     = \"secret\"\n  sensitive = true\n}\n```\n\nWhen we run a plan or apply, the sensitive value is redacted from output:\n\n```\nTerraform will perform the following actions:\n\n  # test_instance.x will be created\n  + resource \"test_instance\" \"x\" {\n      + some_attribute    = (sensitive value)\n    }\n\nPlan: 1 to add, 0 to change, 0 to destroy.\n\nChanges to Outputs:\n  + out = (sensitive value)\n```\n\nTerraform will still record sensitive values in the [state](/terraform/language/state),\nand so anyone who can access the state data will have access to the sensitive\nvalues in cleartext. For more information, see\n[Sensitive Data in State](/terraform/language/state/sensitive-data).\n\n<a id=\"depends_on\"></a>\n\n### `depends_on` \u2014 Explicit Output Dependencies\n\nSince output values are just a means for passing data out of a module, it is\nusually not necessary to worry about their relationships with other nodes in\nthe dependency graph.\n\nHowever, when a parent module accesses an output value exported by one of its\nchild modules, the dependencies of that output value allow Terraform to\ncorrectly determine the dependencies between resources defined in different\nmodules.\n\nJust as with\n[resource dependencies](/terraform/language/resources/behavior#resource-dependencies),\nTerraform analyzes the `value` expression for an output value and automatically\ndetermines a set of dependencies, but in less-common cases there are\ndependencies that cannot be recognized implicitly. In these rare cases, the\n`depends_on` argument can be used to create additional explicit dependencies:\n\n```hcl\noutput \"instance_ip_addr\" {\n  value       = aws_instance.server.private_ip\n  description = \"The private IP address of the main server instance.\"\n\n  depends_on = [\n    # Security group rule must be created before this IP address could\n    # actually be used, otherwise the services will be unreachable.\n    aws_security_group_rule.local_access,\n  ]\n}\n```\n\nThe `depends_on` argument should be used only as a last resort. When using it,\nalways include a comment explaining why it is being used, to help future\nmaintainers understand the purpose of the additional dependency.\n",

The `ephemeral` property is helpful when managing credentials, tokens, or other temporary resources you do not want to store in Terraform state files.\n> \n> Learn more about defining ephemeral [input variables](/terraform/language/values/variables#exclude-values-from-state), [outputs](/terraform/language/values/outputs#ephemeral-avoid-storing-values-in-state-or-plan-files), and [resources](/terraform/language/resources/ephemeral).\n21,22c26\n< If you manage any sensitive data with Terraform (like database passwords, user\n< passwords, or private keys), treat the state itself as sensitive data.\n---\n> Treat the state as sensitive data if you manage secret credentials like database passwords, user passwords, or private keys with Terraform.
