The workflows of `ephemeralasnull` are subject to change.\n\n\nThis topic provides reference information about the `ephemeralasnull` function. The `ephemeralasnull` function accepts an ephemeral value and returns `null`. \n\n## Introduction\n\nYou can use the `ephemeralasnull` function to nullify ephemeral values. For example, if you pass an object with a nested ephemeral value to `ephemeralasnull`,it nullifies any ephemeral values within that object.\n\n## Syntax\n\nUse the `ephemeralasnull` function with the following syntax:\n\n```hcl\nephemeralasnull(var.my_ephemeral_value)\n```\n\nIn the following example, you can pass the `ephemeralasnull` function an ephemeral value, and the function returns `null`:\n\n```hcl\nvariable \"example\" {\n  type      = string\n  default   = \"test\"\n  ephemeral = true\n}\n\n# This output returns null.\noutput \"example_output\" {\n  value       = ephemeralasnull(var.example)\n}\n```\n\nFor more information on which Terraform blocks can be ephemeral, refer to ephemeral [inputs](/terraform/language/values/variables#exclude-values-from-state), [outputs](/terraform/language/values/outputs#ephemeral-avoid-storing-values-in-state-or-plan-files), and [resources](/terraform/language/resources/ephemeral).\n\n## Example use case\n\nThe following example shows how you can use the  `ephemeralasnull` function to intake a map that contains some ephemeral values, and output the values that are not ephemeral.\n\n```hcl\nvariable \"session_token\" {\n  type      = string\n  default   = \"test\"\n  ephemeral = true\n}\n\nvariable \"configuration\" {\n  type = map(string)\n  default = {\n    \"env\" = \"development\"\n  }\n}\n\n# This is a contrived example, but this pattern works with any object that is a mix of ephemeral and non-ephemeral values. \nlocals {\n  configuration_with_token = merge(\n    var.configuration,\n    { \"session_token\" = var.session_token }\n  )\n  ephemeral = true\n}\n\noutput \"configuration_settings\" {\n# Using ephemeralasnull enables you to output the non-ephemeral values.\n  value       = ephemeralasnull(local.things_with_token)\n  description = \"Environment setting.\"\n}\n```\n\nWhen you apply the above configuration, Terraform returns the `ephemeral` values in `locals.configuration_settings` as `null`. This lets you output the non-ephemeral value `env`.\n\n```hcl\nOutputs:\n\nconfiguration_settings = {\n  \"env\" = \"development\"\n  \"session_token\" = tostring(null)\n}\n```",

Ephemerality in Terraform means that the data of a block is available during runtime, but Terraform does not write that data to state or plan files. The `ephemeral` property is helpful when managing credentials, tokens, or other temporary resources you do not want to store in Terraform state files.\n> \n> Learn more about defining ephemeral [input variables](/terraform/language/values/variables#exclude-values-from-state), [outputs](/terraform/language/values/outputs#ephemeral-avoid-storing-values-in-state-or-plan-files), and [resources](/terraform/language/resources/ephemeral).\n21,22c26\n< If you manage any sensitive data with Terraform (like database passwords, user\n< passwords, or private keys), treat the state itself as sensitive data.\n---\n> Treat the state as sensitive data if you manage secret credentials like database passwords, user passwords, or private keys with Terraform. You can also mark your sensitive data in variables as `ephemeral` to prevent Terraform from writing those variables to your state and plan files.\n38c42\n< - The GCS (Google Cloud Storage) backend supports using [customer-supplied](/terraform/language/settings/backends/gcs#customer-supplied-encryption-keys) or [customer-managed (Cloud KMS)](/terraform/language/settings/backends/gcs#customer-managed-encryption-keys-cloud-kms) encryption keys.\n---\n> - The GCS (Google Cloud Storage) backend supports using [customer-supplied](/terraform/language/backend/gcs#customer-supplied-encryption-keys) or [customer-managed (Cloud KMS)](/terraform/language/backend/gcs#customer-managed-encryption-keys-cloud-kms) encryption keys.\n\n=== DIFF: website/docs/language/state/workspaces.mdx ===\n10c10\n< Each Terraform configuration has an associated [backend](/terraform/language/settings/backends/configuration) that defines how Terraform executes operations and where Terraform stores persistent data, like [state](/terraform/language/state/purpose).\n---\n> Each Terraform configuration has an associated [backend](/terraform/language/backend) that defines how Terraform executes operations and where Terraform stores persistent data, like [state](/terraform/language/state/purpose).\n15c15\n< -> **Note**: The Terraform CLI workspaces are different from [workspaces in HCP Terraform](/terraform/cloud-docs/workspaces). Marking an input variable as `ephemeral` is useful for data that only needs to exist temporarily, such as a short-lived token or session identifier.\n> \n> Mark an input variable as ephemeral by setting the `ephemeral` argument to `true`:\n> \n> ```hcl\n> variable \"session_token\" {\n>   type      = string\n>   ephemeral = true\n> }\n> ```\n> \n> Ephemeral variables are available during the current Terraform operation, and Terraform does not store them in state or plan files. So, unlike [`sensitive`](#sensitive) inputs, Terraform ensures ephemeral values are not available beyond the lifetime of the current Terraform run.\n> \n> You can only reference ephemeral variables in specific contexts or Terraform throws an error. The following are valid contexts for referencing ephemeral variables:\n> * Another ephemeral variable\n> * In [local values](/terraform/language/values/locals#ephemeral-values)\n> * In [ephemeral resources](/terraform/language/resources/ephemeral)\n> * In [ephemeral outputs](/terraform/language/values/outputs#ephemeral-avoid-storing-values-in-state-or-plan-files)\n> \n> If another expression references an `ephemeral` variable, that expression implicitly becomes ephemeral.\n> \n> ```hcl\n> variable \"password\" {\n>   type      = string\n>   ephemeral = true\n> }\n> \n> locals {\n>   # local.database_password is implicitly ephemeral because \n>   # var.password is ephemeral.\n>   database_password = var.password\n> }\n> ```\n> \n> The `local.database_password` value is implicitly ephemeral because it depends on `var.password`.\n> \n182,183d224\n< -> This feature was introduced in Terraform v0.14.0.\n< \n192,193c233,234\n< values in cleartext. The workflows of ephemeral outputs are subject to change.\n> \n> You can mark `output` values as `ephemeral` in child modules to pass ephemeral values between modules while avoiding persisting those values to state or plan files. This is useful for managing credentials, tokens, or other temporary resources you do not want to store in Terraform state files.\n> \n> You can mark an `output` in a child module as ephemeral by setting the `ephemeral` attribute to `true`:\n> \n> ```hcl\n> # modules/db/main.tf\n> \n> output \"secret_id\" {\n>   value       = aws_secretsmanager_secret.secret_id\n>   description = \"Temporary secret ID for accessing database in AWS.\"\n>   ephemeral   = true\n> }\n> ```\n> \n> Terraform has access to the value of `output` blocks during plan and apply operations. At the end of a plan or apply operation, Terraform does not persist the value of any ephemeral outputs.\n> \n> You can only reference ephemeral outputs in specific contexts or Terraform throws an error. The following are valid contexts for referencing ephemeral outputs:\n> * In another child module's ephemeral `output` block\n> * In [ephemeral variables](/terraform/language/values/variables#exclude-values-from-state)\n> * In [ephemeral resources](/terraform/language/resources/ephemeral)\n> \n> Note that you cannot set an `output` value as `ephemeral` in the root module.\n> \n> \n171,176d200\n< -> **Note:** In Terraform versions prior to Terraform 0.14, setting an output\n< value in the root module as sensitive would prevent Terraform from showing its\n< value in the list of outputs at the end of `terraform apply`.     "content": "---\npage_title: Conditional Expressions - Configuration Language\ndescription: >-\n  Conditional expressions select one of two values. You can use them to define\n  defaults to replace invalid values.\n---\n\n# Conditional Expressions\n\nA _conditional expression_ uses the value of a boolean expression to select one of\ntwo values.\n\n> **Hands-on:** Try the [Create Dynamic Expressions](/terraform/tutorials/configuration-language/expressions?utm_source=WEBSITE&utm_medium=WEB_IO&utm_offer=ARTICLE_PAGE&utm_content=DOCS) tutorial.\n\n## Syntax\n\nThe syntax of a conditional expression is as follows:\n\n```hcl\ncondition ? true_val : false_val\n```\n\nIf `condition` is `true` then the result is `true_val`. If `condition` is\n`false` then the result is `false_val`.\n\nA common use of conditional expressions is to define defaults to replace\ninvalid values:\n\n```hcl\nvar.a != \"\" ? var.a : \"default-a\"\n```\n\nIf `var.a` is an empty string then the result is `\"default-a\"`, but otherwise\nit is the actual value of `var.a`.\n\n## Conditions\n\nThe condition can be any expression that resolves to a boolean value. This will\nusually be an expression that uses the equality, comparison, or logical\noperators.\n\n### Custom Condition Checks\n\nYou can create conditions that produce custom error messages for several types of objects in a configuration. For example, you can add a condition to an input variable that checks whether incoming image IDs are formatted properly.\n\nCustom conditions can help capture assumptions, helping future maintainers understand the configuration design and intent. They also return useful information about errors earlier and in context, helping consumers more easily diagnose issues in their configurations.\n\nRefer to [Custom Condition Checks](/terraform/language/expressions/custom-conditions#input-variable-validation) for details.\n\n## Result Types\n\nThe two result values may be of any type, but they must both\nbe of the _same_ type so that Terraform can determine what type the whole\nconditional expression will return without knowing the condition value.\n\nIf the two result expressions don't produce the same type then Terraform will\nattempt to find a type that they can both convert to, and make those\nconversions automatically if so.\n\nFor example, the following expression is valid and will always return a string,\nbecause in Terraform all numbers can convert automatically to a string using\ndecimal digits:\n\n```hcl\nvar.example ? 12 : \"hello\"\n```\n\nRelying on this automatic conversion behavior can be confusing for those who\nare not familiar with Terraform's conversion rules though, so we recommend\nbeing explicit using type conversion functions in any situation where there may\nbe some uncertainty about the expected result type.\n\nThe following example is contrived because it would be easier to write the\nconstant `\"12\"` instead of the type conversion in this case, but shows how to\nuse [`tostring`](/terraform/language/functions/tostring) to explicitly convert a number to\na string.\n\n```hcl\nvar.example ? tostring(12) : \"hello\"\n```\n",

The Terraform state is written to the key `path/to/my/key`.\n\nNote that for the access credentials we recommend using a [partial configuration](/terraform/language/backend#partial-configuration).\n\n## State Storage\n\nThe S3 backend stores state data in an S3 object at the path set by the `key` parameter in the S3 bucket indicated by the `bucket` parameter.\nUsing the example shown above, the state would be stored at the path `path/to/my/key` in the bucket `mybucket`.\n\nWhen using [workspaces](/terraform/language/state/workspaces), the state for the `default` workspace is stored at the location described above.\nOther workspaces are stored using the path `<workspace_key_prefix>/<workspace_name>/<key>`.\nThe default workspace key prefix is `env:` and it can be configured using the parameter `workspace_key_prefix`.\nUsing the example above, the state for the workspace `development` would be stored at the path `env:/development/path/to/my/key`.\n\n## Permissions Required\n\n### S3 Bucket Permissions\n\nWhen not using [workspaces](/terraform/language/state/workspaces)(or when only using the `default` workspace), Terraform will need the following AWS IAM permissions on the target backend bucket:\n\n* `s3:ListBucket` on `arn:aws:s3:::mybucket`. At a minimum, this must be able to list the path where the state is stored.\n* `s3:GetObject` on `arn:aws:s3:::mybucket/path/to/my/key`\n* `s3:PutObject` on `arn:aws:s3:::mybucket/path/to/my/key`\n\nNote: `s3:DeleteObject` is not needed, as Terraform will not delete the state storage.\n\nThis is seen in the following AWS IAM Statement:\n\n```json\n{\n  \"Version\": \"2012-10-17\",\n  \"Statement\": [\n    {\n      \"Effect\": \"Allow\",\n      \"Action\": \"s3:ListBucket\",\n      \"Resource\": \"arn:aws:s3:::mybucket\"\n    },\n    {\n      \"Effect\": \"Allow\",\n      \"Action\": [\"s3:GetObject\", \"s3:PutObject\"],\n      \"Resource\": \"arn:aws:s3:::mybucket/path/to/my/key\"\n    }\n  ]\n}\n```\n\nWhen using [workspaces](/terraform/language/state/workspaces), Terraform will also need permissions to create, list, read, update, and delete the workspace state storage:\n\n* `s3:ListBucket` on `arn:aws:s3:::mybucket`. At a minumum, this must be able to list the path where the `default` workspace is stored as well as the other workspaces.\n* `s3:GetObject` on `arn:aws:s3:::mybucket/path/to/my/key` and `arn:aws:s3:::mybucket/<workspace_key_prefix>/*/path/to/my/key`\n* `s3:PutObject` on `arn:aws:s3:::mybucket/path/to/my/key` and `arn:aws:s3:::mybucket/<workspace_key_prefix>/*/path/to/my/key`\n* `s3:DeleteObject` on `arn:aws:s3:::mybucket/<workspace_key_prefix>/*/path/to/my/key`\n\n-> **Note:** AWS can control access to S3 buckets with either IAM policies\nattached to users/groups/roles (like the example above) or resource policies\nattached to bucket objects (which look similar but also require a `Principal` to\nindicate which entity has those permissions). An\nexample output might look like:\n\n```\ndata.terraform_remote_state.network:\n  id = 2016-10-29 01:57:59.780010914 +0000 UTC\n  addresses.# = 2\n  addresses.0 = 52.207.220.222\n  addresses.1 = 54.196.78.166\n  backend = s3\n  config.% = 3\n  config.bucket = terraform-state-prod\n  config.key = network/terraform.tfstate\n  config.region = us-east-1\n  elb_address = web-elb-790251200.us-east-1.elb.amazonaws.com\n  public_subnet_id = subnet-1e05dd33\n```\n\n## Configuration\n\nThis backend requires the configuration of the AWS Region and S3 state storage. Other configuration, such as enabling DynamoDB state locking, is optional.\n\n### Credentials and Shared Configuration\n\n!> **Warning:**  We recommend using environment variables to supply credentials and other sensitive data. If you use `-backend-config` or hardcode these values directly in your configuration, Terraform will include these values in both the `.terraform` subdirectory and in plan files. Refer to [Credentials and Sensitive Data](/terraform/language/backend#credentials-and-sensitive-data) for details.\n\nThe following configuration is required:\n\n* `region` - (Required) AWS Region of the S3 Bucket and DynamoDB Table (if used). This can also be sourced from the `AWS_DEFAULT_REGION` and `AWS_REGION` environment variables.\n\nThe following configuration is optional:\n\n* `use_lockfile` - (Experimental, Optional) Whether to use a lockfile for locking the state file. Defaults to `false`.\n* `access_key` - (Optional) AWS access key. If configured, must also configure `secret_key`. This can also be sourced from the `AWS_ACCESS_KEY_ID` environment variable, AWS shared credentials file (e.g. `~/.aws/credentials`), or AWS shared configuration file (e.g. `~/.aws/config`).\n* `allowed_account_ids` - (Optional) List of allowed AWS account IDs to prevent potential destruction of a live environment. Conflicts with `forbidden_account_ids`.\n* `custom_ca_bundle` - (Optional) File containing custom root and intermediate certificates. Can also be set using the `AWS_CA_BUNDLE` environment variable. Setting ca_bundle in the shared config file is not supported.\n* `ec2_metadata_service_endpoint` - (Optional) Custom endpoint URL for the EC2 Instance Metadata Service (IMDS) API.\n  Can also be set with the `AWS_EC2_METADATA_SERVICE_ENDPOINT` environment variable.\n* `ec2_metadata_service_endpoint_mode` - (Optional) Mode to use in communicating with the metadata service.\n  Valid values are `IPv4` and `IPv6`.\n  Can also be set with the `AWS_EC2_METADATA_SERVICE_ENDPOINT_MODE` environment variable.\n* `forbidden_account_ids` - (Optional) List of forbidden AWS account IDs to prevent potential destruction of a live environment. Conflicts with `allowed_account_ids`.\n* `http_proxy` - (Optional) URL of a proxy to use for HTTP requests when accessing the AWS API.\n  Can also be set using the `HTTP_PROXY` or `http_proxy` environment variables.\n* `https_proxy` - (Optional) URL of a proxy to use for HTTPS requests when accessing the AWS API.\n  Can also be set using the `HTTPS_PROXY` or `https_proxy` environment variables.\n* `iam_endpoint` - (Optional, **Deprecated**) Custom endpoint URL for the AWS Identity and Access Management (IAM) API.\n  Use `endpoints.iam` instead.\n* `insecure` - (Optional) Whether to explicitly allow the backend to perform \"insecure\" SSL requests. If omitted, the default value is `false`.\n* `no_proxy` - (Optional) Comma-separated list of hosts that should not use HTTP or HTTPS proxies.\n  Each value can be one of:\n    * A domain name\n    * An IP address\n    * A CIDR address\n    * An asterisk (`*`), to indicate that no proxying should be performed\n  Domain name and IP address values can also include a port number.\n  Can also be set using the `NO_PROXY` or `no_proxy` environment variables.\n* `max_retries` - (Optional) The maximum number of times an AWS API request is retried on retryable failure. Defaults to 5.\n* `profile` - (Optional) Name of AWS profile in AWS shared credentials file (e.g. `~/.aws/credentials`) or AWS shared configuration file (e.g. `~/.aws/config`) to use for credentials and/or configuration. This can also be sourced from the `AWS_PROFILE` environment variable.\n* `retry_mode` - (Optional) Specifies how retries are attempted. Valid values are `standard` and `adaptive`. Can also be configured using the `AWS_RETRY_MODE` environment variable or the shared config file parameter `retry_mode`.\n* `secret_key` - (Optional) AWS access key. If configured, must also configure `access_key`. This can also be sourced from the `AWS_SECRET_ACCESS_KEY` environment variable, AWS shared credentials file (e.g. `~/.aws/credentials`), or AWS shared configuration file (e.g. `~/.aws/config`).\n* `shared_config_files`  - (Optional) List of paths to AWS shared configuration files. Defaults to `~/.aws/config`.\n* `shared_credentials_file`  - (Optional, **Deprecated**, use `shared_credentials_files` instead) Path to the AWS shared credentials file. Defaults to `~/.aws/credentials`.\n* `shared_credentials_files`  - (Optional) List of paths to AWS shared credentials files. Defaults to `~/.aws/credentials`.\n* `skip_credentials_validation` - (Optional) Skip credentials validation via the STS API.\n  Useful for testing and for AWS API implementations that do not have STS available.\n* `skip_region_validation` - (Optional) Skip validation of provided region name.\n* `skip_requesting_account_id` - (Optional) Whether to skip requesting the account ID.\n  Useful for AWS API implementations that do not have the IAM, STS API, or metadata API.\n* `skip_metadata_api_check` - (Optional) Skip usage of EC2 Metadata API.\n* `skip_s3_checksum` - (Optional) Do not include checksum when uploading S3 Objects.\n  Useful for some S3-Compatible APIs.\n* `sts_endpoint` - (Optional, **Deprecated**) Custom endpoint URL for the AWS Security Token Service (STS) API.\n  Use `endpoints.sts` instead.\n* `sts_region` - (Optional) AWS region for STS. If unset, AWS will use the same region for STS as other non-STS operations.\n* `token` - (Optional) Multi-Factor Authentication (MFA) token. This can also be sourced from the `AWS_SESSION_TOKEN` environment variable.\n* `use_dualstack_endpoint` - (Optional) Force the backend to resolve endpoints with DualStack capability. Can also be set with the `AWS_USE_DUALSTACK_ENDPOINT` environment variable or in a shared config file (`use_dualstack_endpoint`).\n* `use_fips_endpoint` - (Optional) Force the backend to resolve endpoints with FIPS capability. Can also be set with the `AWS_USE_FIPS_ENDPOINT` environment variable or in a shared config file (`use_fips_endpoint`).\n\n#### Overriding AWS API endpoints\n\nThe optional argument `endpoints` contains the following arguments:\n\n* `dynamodb` - (Optional) Custom endpoint URL for the AWS DynamoDB API.\n  This can also be sourced from the environment variable `AWS_ENDPOINT_URL_DYNAMODB` or the deprecated environment variable `AWS_DYNAMODB_ENDPOINT`.\n* `iam` - (Optional) Custom endpoint URL for the AWS IAM API.\n  This can also be sourced from the environment variable `AWS_ENDPOINT_URL_IAM` or the deprecated environment variable `AWS_IAM_ENDPOINT`.\n* `s3` - (Optional) Custom endpoint URL for the AWS S3 API.\n  This can also be sourced from the environment variable `AWS_ENDPOINT_URL_S3` or the deprecated environment variable `AWS_S3_ENDPOINT`.\n* `sso` - (Optional) Custom endpoint URL for the AWS IAM Identity Center (formerly known as AWS SSO) API.\n  This can also be sourced from the environment variable `AWS_ENDPOINT_URL_SSO`.\n* `sts` - (Optional) Custom endpoint URL for the AWS STS API.\n  This can also be sourced from the environment variable `AWS_ENDPOINT_URL_STS` or the deprecated environment variable `AWS_STS_ENDPOINT`.\n\nThe environment variable `AWS_ENDPOINT_URL` can be used to set a base endpoint URL for all services.\n\nEndpoints can also be overridden using the [AWS shared configuration file](https://docs.aws.amazon.com/sdkref/latest/guide/feature-ss-endpoints.html#ss-endpoints-config).\nSetting the parameter `endpoint_url` on a profile will set that endpoint for all services.\nTo set endpoints for specific services, create a `services` section and set the `endpoint_url` parameters for each desired service.\nEndpoints set for specific services will override the base endpoint configured in the profile.\n\n#### Assume Role Configuration\n\nThe argument `assume_role` contains the following arguments:\n\n* `role_arn` - (Required) Amazon Resource Name (ARN) of the IAM Role to assume.\n* `duration` - (Optional) The duration individual credentials will be valid.\n  Credentials are automatically renewed up to the maximum defined by the AWS account.\n  Specified using the format `<hours>h<minutes>m<seconds>s` with any unit being optional.\n  For example, an hour and a half can be specified as `1h30m` or `90m`. \n  Must be between 15 minutes (15m) and 12 hours (12h).\n* `external_id` - (Optional) External identifier to use when assuming the role.\n* `policy` - (Optional) IAM Policy JSON describing further restricting permissions for the IAM Role being assumed.\n* `policy_arns` - (Optional) Set of Amazon Resource Names (ARNs) of IAM Policies describing further restricting permissions for the IAM Role being assumed.\n* `session_name` - (Optional) Session name to use when assuming the role.\n* `source_identity` - (Optional) Source identity specified by the principal assuming the role.\n* `tags` - (Optional) Map of assume role session tags.\n* `transitive_tag_keys` - (Optional) Set of assume role session tag keys to pass to any subsequent sessions.\n\nMultiple `assume_role` values can be specified, and the roles will be assumed in order.\n\n```terraform\nterraform {\n  backend \"s3\" {\n    bucket = \"example-bucket\"\n    key    = \"path/to/state\"\n    region = \"us-east-1\"\n    assume_role = {\n      role_arn = \"arn:aws:iam::PRODUCTION-ACCOUNT-ID:role/Terraform\"\n    }\n  }\n}\n```\n\n#### Assume Role With Web Identity Configuration\n\nThe following `assume_role_with_web_identity` configuration block is optional:\n\n* `role_arn` - (Optional) Session name to use when assuming the role.\n  Can also be set with the `AWS_ROLE_SESSION_NAME` environment variable.\n* `web_identity_token` - (Optional) The value of a web identity token from an OpenID Connect (OIDC) or OAuth provider.\n  One of `web_identity_token` or `web_identity_token_file` is required.\n* `web_identity_token_file` - (Optional) File containing a web identity token from an OpenID Connect (OIDC) or OAuth provider.\n  One of `web_identity_token_file` or `web_identity_token` is required.\n  Can also be set with the `AWS_WEB_IDENTITY_TOKEN_FILE` environment variable.\n\n```terraform\nterraform {\n  backend \"s3\" {\n    bucket = \"example-bucket\"\n    key    = \"path/to/state\"\n    region = \"us-east-1\"\n    assume_role_with_web_identity = {\n      role_arn           = \"arn:aws:iam::PRODUCTION-ACCOUNT-ID:role/Terraform\"",

Defaults to `false`.\n\nTo enable DynamoDB state locking, use the following optional arguments:\n\n* `dynamodb_endpoint` - (Optional, **Deprecated**) Custom endpoint URL for the AWS DynamoDB API.\n  Use `endpoints.dynamodb` instead.\n* `dynamodb_table` - (Optional) Name of DynamoDB Table to use for state locking and consistency. The table must have a partition key named `LockID` with type of `String`.\n\n## Multi-account AWS Architecture\n\nA common architectural pattern is for an organization to use a number of\nseparate AWS accounts to isolate different teams and environments. For example,\na \"staging\" system will often be deployed into a separate AWS account than\nits corresponding \"production\" system, to minimize the risk of the staging\nenvironment affecting production infrastructure, whether via rate limiting,\nmisconfigured access controls, or other unintended interactions.\n\nThe S3 backend can be used in a number of different ways that make different\ntradeoffs between convenience, security, and isolation in such an organization.\nThis section describes one such approach that aims to find a good compromise\nbetween these tradeoffs, allowing use of\n[Terraform's workspaces feature](/terraform/language/state/workspaces) to switch\nconveniently between multiple isolated deployments of the same configuration.\n\nUse this section as a starting-point for your approach, but note that\nyou will probably need to make adjustments for the unique standards and\nregulations that apply to your organization. You will also need to make some\nadjustments to this approach to account for _existing_ practices within your\norganization, if for example other tools have previously been used to manage\ninfrastructure.\n\nTerraform is an administrative tool that manages your infrastructure, and so\nideally the infrastructure that is used by Terraform should exist outside of\nthe infrastructure that Terraform manages. This can be achieved by creating a\nseparate _administrative_ AWS account which contains the user accounts used by\nhuman operators and any infrastructure and tools used to manage the other\naccounts. Isolating shared administrative tools from your main environments\nhas a number of advantages, such as avoiding accidentally damaging the\nadministrative infrastructure while changing the target infrastructure, and\nreducing the risk that an attacker might abuse production infrastructure to\ngain access to the (usually more privileged) administrative infrastructure.\n\n### Administrative Account Setup\n\nYour administrative AWS account will contain at least the following items:\n\n* One or more [IAM user](https://docs.aws.amazon.com/IAM/latest/UserGuide/id_users.html)\n  for system administrators that will log in to maintain infrastructure in\n  the other accounts.\n* Optionally, one or more [IAM groups](https://docs.aws.amazon.com/IAM/latest/UserGuide/id_groups.html)\n  to differentiate between different groups of users that have different\n  levels of access to the other AWS accounts.\n* An [S3 bucket](https://docs.aws.amazon.com/AmazonS3/latest/userguide/UsingBucket.html)\n  that will contain the Terraform state files for each workspace.\n* A [DynamoDB table](https://docs.aws.amazon.com/amazondynamodb/latest/developerguide/HowItWorks.CoreComponents.html#HowItWorks.CoreComponents.TablesItemsAttributes)\n  that will be used for locking to prevent concurrent operations on a single\n  workspace.\n\nProvide the S3 bucket name and DynamoDB table name to Terraform within the\nS3 backend configuration using the `bucket` and `dynamodb_table` arguments\nrespectively, and configure a suitable `workspace_key_prefix` to contain\nthe states of the various workspaces that will subsequently be created for\nthis configuration.\n\n### Environment Account Setup",

Input
{
  "content": "# Terraform v1.9.0 to v1.10.0 Migration Guide\n\n## Overview\n\nTerraform v1.10.0 is a minor release in the stable v1.0 series and honors the [Terraform v1.0 Compatibility Promises](https://developer.hashicorp.com/terraform/language/v1-compatibility-promises). However, this release includes breaking changes in the S3 backend configuration and `moved` block syntax that require manual action before or during upgrade, as well as important behavioral changes to sensitive value propagation and a significant new feature (ephemeral resources and values) that changes how secret data should be handled going forward.\n\n**Key themes:**\n- **Breaking:** S3 backend removes deprecated root-level IAM role assumption attributes\n- **Breaking:** `moved` blocks now require `resource.` prefix for types that conflict with reserved keywords\n- **Behavioral:** Sensitive marks now consistently propagate through conditional and `for` expressions\n- **New:** Ephemeral resources, input variables, and outputs for secret handling without state persistence\n\n---\n\n## Breaking Changes\n\n### 1. S3 Backend: Removal of Root-Level IAM Role Assumption Attributes\n\n#### What Changed and Why\n\nSeveral root-level attributes for IAM role assumption in the S3 backend that were previously deprecated have been **permanently removed** in v1.10.0. These attributes have had block-based replacements since earlier Terraform versions, and the migration was deferred until this release.\n\nThe removed attributes and their replacements are:\n\n| Removed Attribute | Replacement (inside `assume_role` block) |\n|---|---|\n| `role_arn` | `assume_role.role_arn` |\n| `session_name` | `assume_role.session_name` |\n| `external_id` | `assume_role.external_id` |\n| `assume_role_duration_seconds` | `assume_role.duration` |\n| `assume_role_policy` | `assume_role.policy` |\n| `assume_role_policy_arns` | `assume_role.policy_arns` |\n| `assume_role_tags` | `assume_role.tags` |\n| `assume_role_transitive_tag_keys` | `assume_role.transitive_tag_keys` |\n\nNote that `assume_role_duration_seconds` maps to `duration` (not `duration_seconds`) in the block syntax, and accepts a duration string (e.g. `\"1h30m\"`) rather than an integer number of seconds.\n\n#### Before/After Code Examples\n\n**Before (v1.9.0 \u2014 using deprecated root-level attributes):**\n\n```hcl\nterraform {\n  backend \"s3\" {\n    bucket       = \"my-terraform-state\"\n    key          = \"prod/terraform.tfstate\"\n    region       = \"us-east-1\"\n\n    # These root-level attributes are REMOVED in v1.10.0\n    role_arn                   = \"arn:aws:iam::123456789012:role/TerraformRole\"\n    session_name               = \"terraform-session\"\n    external_id                = \"my-external-id\"\n    assume_role_duration_seconds = 3600\n    assume_role_policy         = \"{\\\"Version\\\":\\\"2012-10-17\\\",\\\"Statement\\\":[]}\"\n    assume_role_policy_arns    = [\"arn:aws:iam::aws:policy/ReadOnlyAccess\"]\n    assume_role_tags           = { Environment = \"prod\" }\n    assume_role_transitive_tag_keys = [\"Environment\"]\n  }\n}\n```\n\n**After (v1.10.0 \u2014 using `assume_role` block):**\n\n```hcl\nterraform {\n  backend \"s3\" {\n    bucket = \"my-terraform-state\"\n    key    = \"prod/terraform.tfstate\"\n    region = \"us-east-1\"\n\n    assume_role = {\n      role_arn               = \"arn:aws:iam::123456789012:role/TerraformRole\"\n      session_name           = \"terraform-session\"\n      external_id            = \"my-external-id\"\n      duration               = \"1h\"   # Note: string format, not seconds integer\n      policy                 = \"{\\\"Version\\\":\\\"2012-10-17\\\",\\\"Statement\\\":[]}\"\n      policy_arns            = [\"arn:aws:iam::aws:policy/ReadOnlyAccess\"]\n      tags                   = { Environment = \"prod\" }\n      transitive_tag_keys    = [\"Environment\"]\n    }\n  }\n}\n```\n\n#### Step-by-Step Migration Instructions\n\n1. **Identify affected configurations:** Search all your Terraform configuration files for any `backend \"s3\"` blocks that contain root-level `role_arn`, `session_name`, `external_id`, `assume_role_duration_seconds`, `assume_role_policy`, `assume_role_policy_arns`, `assume_role_tags`, or `assume_role_transitive_tag_keys` attributes.\n\n   ```bash\n   grep -rn \"role_arn\\|session_name\\|external_id\\|assume_role_duration_seconds\\|assume_role_policy\\|assume_role_tags\\|assume_role_transitive_tag_keys\" . --include=\"*.tf\" --include=\"*.tfbackend\"\n   ```\n\n2. **Update each backend configuration:** Move all the matching root-level attributes inside an `assume_role = { ... }` block. Convert `assume_role_duration_seconds` (integer) to `duration` (duration string, e.g. `3600` seconds becomes `\"1h\"`).\n\n3. **Handle partial configurations:** If your backend uses `-backend-config` flags or partial configuration files (`.tfbackend`), update those files as well. The block syntax must be used in all forms.\n\n4. **Re-initialize:** After updating the configuration, run `terraform init` to validate the new backend configuration:\n\n   ```bash\n   terraform init\n   ```\n\n5. **Verify:** Confirm that `terraform plan` runs without errors.\n\n#### Common Pitfalls\n\n- **Duration format change:** `assume_role_duration_seconds` accepted an integer (e.g. `3600`), but `duration` in the `assume_role` block requires a duration string (e.g. `\"1h\"`, `\"90m\"`, `\"1h30m\"`). Forgetting this conversion will cause a validation error.\n- **Partial configuration files:** Backend configuration supplied via `-backend-config` files must also be updated to use the block syntax.\n- **Multiple `assume_role` blocks:** The `assume_role` argument supports multiple values in v1.10.0 \u2014 roles are assumed in order. If you were chaining role assumptions, you can now express this as a list.\n\n---\n\n### 2. `moved` Blocks: Required `resource.` Prefix for Reserved Type Names\n\n#### What Changed and Why\n\nIn v1.10.0, `moved` blocks now correctly parse resource addresses using the same rules as the rest of the Terraform language. This means reserved keywords and top-level block types (such as `local`, `each`, `self`, `count`, `path`, `module`, `var`, `data`, `resource`, `provider`, `terraform`, `check`, and similar) are now recognized as keywords rather than resource type names when they appear as the first segment of an address in `from` or `to` fields.\n\nIf you have a resource with a **type name** that matches one of these reserved words (e.g., `resource \"local\" \"config\" {}`), you must now use the `resource.` prefix in any `moved` block that references it.\n\n#### Before/After Code Examples\n\n**Scenario:** A resource with type `local` (an unusual but valid type name from a custom provider).\n\n**Before (v1.9.0 \u2014 worked, now errors in v1.10.0):**\n\n```hcl\n# Resource definition\nresource \"local\" \"config\" {\n  # ...\n}\n\n# This moved block previously parsed \"local.config\" as resource type \"local\", name \"config\"\nmoved {\n  from = local.config\n  to   = local.settings\n}\n```\n\n**After (v1.10.0 \u2014 must use `resource.` prefix):**\n\n```hcl\n# Resource definition\nresource \"local\" \"config\" {\n  # ...\n}\n\n# Must now explicitly qualify with \"resource.\" when the type matches a keyword\nmoved {\n  from = resource.local.config\n  to   = resource.local.settings\n}\n```\n\n#### Step-by-Step Migration Instructions\n\n1. **Identify affected `moved` blocks:** Search for `moved` blocks in your configurations and check whether the resource type name in the `from` or `to` fields matches any reserved keyword.\n\n   Reserved keywords include (but are not limited to): `local`, `locals`, `each`, `self`, `count`, `path`, `module`, `var`, `data`, `resource`, `provider`, `terraform`, `check`, `import`, `removed`, `moved`.\n\n   ```bash\n   grep -rn \"moved {\" . --include=\"*.tf\" -A 5\n   ```\n\n2. **Check for conflicts:** For each `moved` block, examine the `from` and `to` addresses. If the first segment (the type name) matches a reserved word, add the `resource.` prefix.\n\n3. **Test with `terraform validate`:** Run `terraform validate` after updating to confirm there are no remaining errors:\n\n   ```bash\n   terraform validate\n   ```\n\n4. **Run a plan to confirm:** Execute `terraform plan` to verify that Terraform correctly identifies the moved resources and proposes no unexpected changes:\n\n   ```bash\n   terraform plan\n   ```\n\n#### Common Pitfalls\n\n- **Custom providers with generic type names:** Some older or community providers use type names like `local`, `data`, or `module` which can conflict. These configurations need the `resource.` qualifier.\n- **Module-level `moved` blocks:** This applies to `moved` blocks in both root modules and child modules. Check all modules in your configuration.\n- **The `resource.` prefix is always valid:** Even in cases where the prefix was not previously required, adding `resource.` to a resource address in a `moved` block is valid in all Terraform v1.x versions. If you are unsure whether a type name conflicts, adding the prefix is safe.\n\n---\n\n## Behavioral Changes\n\n### 3. Sensitive Value Handling: Consistent Mark Propagation in Expressions\n\n#### What Changed and Why\n\nPrior to v1.10.0, there was a bug where sensitive marks (the internal mechanism that causes Terraform to redact values shown as `(sensitive value)`) were **incorrectly dropped** in certain conditional expressions (`condition ? true_val : false_val`) and `for` expressions when one of the branches or elements involved an unknown value. This could cause two problems:\n\n1. **Mark loss:** A value that should have been marked sensitive was silently treated as non-sensitive, potentially exposing secret data in plan output.\n2. **Erroneous plan changes:** Sensitive marks were sometimes incorrectly transferred to unrelated nested resource values, causing Terraform to plan spurious changes.\n\nIn v1.10.0, both issues are fixed. Sensitive marks now propagate correctly through all expression types. The result is:\n\n- Expressions that involve a sensitive value will consistently produce a sensitive result.\n- You may see **previously hidden** plan changes become visible now that incorrect mark propagation no longer masks them.\n- Some expressions that previously appeared non-sensitive may now correctly appear as `(sensitive value)` in plan output.\n\n#### When Sensitive Marks Are Now Preserved\n\nIn v1.10.0, the sensitive mark is preserved in all of the following cases (some of which previously dropped the mark):\n\n```hcl\n# If var.secret is sensitive, the result of a conditional expression\n# is now consistently marked sensitive, even when one branch is unknown.\nlocal.value = var.some_condition ? var.secret : \"default\"\n\n# In for expressions, sensitivity is properly propagated\nlocal.filtered = [for item in var.sensitive_list : item if item != \"\"]\n\n# Conditional with unknown condition is now correctly sensitive\nlocal.result = var.unknown_flag ? var.secret : var.other_secret\n```\n\n#### How to Use `nonsensitive()` to Override When Needed\n\nIf the consistent propagation causes `(sensitive value)` to appear where you know the resulting value is safe to expose, use the `nonsensitive()` function to explicitly unwrap the sensitivity mark:\n\n```hcl\n# Before v1.10.0: this might have accidentally exposed the value as non-sensitive\n# After v1.10.0: the result is correctly marked sensitive\nlocal.public_info = var.is_public ? var.api_endpoint : var.secret_endpoint\n\n# If you are certain the output is safe to display, use nonsensitive()\noutput \"safe_endpoint\" {\n  value = nonsensitive(var.is_public ? var.api_endpoint : var.secret_endpoint)\n}\n```\n\n**Only use `nonsensitive()` when you are certain the value does not contain sensitive data.** Misuse can expose secret values in plan output, state files, and logs.\n\n#### Migration Steps\n\n1. **Run `terraform plan` after upgrading** and review any new `(sensitive value)` annotations or any plan changes that were previously hidden. These may indicate legitimate changes that were previously masked.\n\n2. **Review output blocks** that reference conditional expressions involving sensitive variables. If the output is now unexpectedly marked sensitive, you have two options:\n   - Declare the output as `sensitive = true` to explicitly acknowledge it.\n   - Restructure the expression to avoid mixing sensitive and non-sensitive paths, or use `nonsensitive()` if appropriate.\n\n3. **Review resource configurations** that produce unexpected plan changes. If plan changes disappear after upgrade, they may have been artifacts of the previous incorrect mark propagation behavior. Conversely, new plan changes may reflect previously masked real differences.\n\n#### Common Pitfalls\n\n- **`nonsensitive()` does not remove sensitivity from state:** The `nonsensitive()` function only affects plan/apply output display. Sensitive values are still stored in state unless you use ephemeral values (see below).\n- **Unexpected plan diffs:** If you see new plan changes after upgrading that relate to sensitive resource attributes, investigate whether the change is real or was previously masked. Consider using `terraform show` or `terraform state show` to examine the current state.\n\n---\n\n## New Features with Migration Impact\n\n### 4. Ephemeral Resources and Values\n\nTerraform v1.10.0 introduces **ephemeral resources, ephemeral input variables, and ephemeral outputs** as a new mechanism for handling secrets that should never be persisted to state or plan files. This is a public beta feature and the workflows may evolve in future releases.\n\n> **Note:** Ephemeral resources, variables, and outputs are in public beta in v1.10.0. Provider support for ephemeral resources requires providers that implement the new ephemeral resource type.\n\n#### What Is Ephemeral?\n\nEphemeral values exist only in memory during a single Terraform operation (plan or apply). Unlike `sensitive` values, which are stored in state (just redacted in output), **ephemeral values are never written to state files, plan files, or logs**.\n\nUse ephemeral values for:\n- Short-lived authentication tokens (e.g. Vault leases, OIDC tokens)\n- Database passwords that should not persist in state\n- API keys that rotate frequently\n- Any credential that should exist only for the duration of a Terraform run\n\n#### Ephemeral Input Variables\n\nMark an input variable as ephemeral to prevent it from being stored in state:\n\n```hcl\nvariable \"db_password\" {\n  type      = string\n  ephemeral = true\n  sensitive = true  # Also mark sensitive to suppress CLI output\n}\n```\n\nEphemeral variables can only be referenced in:\n- Other ephemeral variables\n- Local values (which become implicitly ephemeral)\n- Ephemeral resources\n- Ephemeral outputs\n- Provider configuration blocks\n- Provisioner and connection blocks\n\n**If you reference an ephemeral variable in a non-ephemeral context, Terraform will return an error.**\n\n#### Ephemeral Outputs (Child Modules Only)\n\nMark an output in a **child module** as ephemeral to pass secrets between modules without persisting them:\n\n```hcl\n# modules/db/main.tf\noutput \"connection_string\" {\n  value     = \"postgres://${var.username}:${var.db_password}@${var.host}:5432/${var.database}\"\n  ephemeral = true\n  sensitive = true\n}\n```\n\n> **Important:** Ephemeral outputs are only supported in child modules. You cannot mark a root module output as `ephemeral`.\n\n#### Ephemeral Resources (Provider Support Required)\n\nProviders that support ephemeral resources expose them via a new `ephemeral` block type. Ephemeral resources are opened, optionally renewed, and closed during each Terraform operation \u2014 their data is never stored in state:\n\n```hcl\n# Retrieve a secret from AWS Secrets Manager without storing it in state\nephemeral \"aws_secretsmanager_secret_version\" \"db_credentials\" {\n  secret_id = data.aws_db_instance.example.master_user_secret[0].secret_arn\n}\n\nlocals {\n  credentials = jsondecode(ephemeral.aws_secretsmanager_secret_version.db_credentials.secret_string)\n}\n\nprovider \"postgresql\" {\n  host     = data.aws_db_instance.example.address\n  port     = data.aws_db_instance.example.port\n  username = local.credentials[\"username\"]\n  password = local.credentials[\"password\"]\n}\n```\n\n#### The `ephemeralasnull` Function\n\nA new built-in function `ephemeralasnull()` converts ephemeral values to `null`, allowing you to output or use the non-ephemeral parts of a mixed object:\n\n```hcl\nvariable \"session_token\" {\n  type      = string\n  ephemeral = true\n}\n\nvariable \"environment\" {\n  type    = string\n  default = \"production\"\n}\n\nlocals {\n  config = {\n    environment   = var.environment\n    session_token = var.session_token  # ephemeral\n  }\n}\n\n# Output only the non-ephemeral parts; session_token becomes null\noutput \"safe_config\" {\n  value = ephemeralasnull(local.config)\n}\n```\n\n#### Migration Impact: Sensitive vs. Ephemeral\n\nBefore v1.10.0, the only way to protect secrets was `sensitive = true`, which hides values in output but **still stores them in state**. Starting with v1.10.0, you have a stronger guarantee with ephemeral values:\n\n| Property | `sensitive = true` | `ephemeral = true` |\n|---|---|---|\n| Hidden in plan/apply output | Yes | Yes (never shown) |\n| Stored in state file | **Yes** | **No** |\n| Stored in plan file | **Yes** | **No** |\n| Available in next run from state | Yes | No (must be re-provided) |\n| Can be used in regular resources | Yes | No (ephemeral contexts only) |\n\n**When to use which:**\n- Use `sensitive` for values that must persist in state (e.g., resource IDs that happen to be sensitive)\n- Use `ephemeral` for credentials, tokens, and passwords that should never touch state\n\n#### IAM and Bucket Policy Updates for S3 Locking\n\nWhen enabling S3 native state locking (`use_lockfile = true`), the lock file is placed alongside the state file with a `.tflock` extension. Update your S3 bucket policies and IAM policies to include permissions for the lock file path:\n\n```json\n{\n  \"Version\": \"2012-10-17\",\n  \"Statement\": [\n    {\n      \"Effect\": \"Allow\",\n      \"Action\": [\"s3:GetObject\", \"s3:PutObject\", \"s3:DeleteObject\"],\n      \"Resource\": [\n        \"arn:aws:s3:::mybucket/path/to/my/key\",\n        \"arn:aws:s3:::mybucket/path/to/my/key.tflock\"\n      ]\n    }\n  ]\n}\n```\n\n#### Opting Into S3 Native State Locking\n\nS3 native locking is opt-in and experimental in v1.10.0. To enable it:\n\n```hcl\nterraform {\n  backend \"s3\" {\n    bucket   = \"my-terraform-state\"\n    key      = \"prod/terraform.tfstate\"\n    region   = \"us-east-1\"\n\n    # Experimental S3 native locking (opt-in)\n    use_lockfile = true\n\n    # DynamoDB locking can run alongside S3 locking during transition\n    dynamodb_table = \"terraform-state-lock\"\n  }\n}\n```\n\nWhen both `use_lockfile` and `dynamodb_table` are configured, **both locks must be acquired** before an operation proceeds. This allows a gradual migration from DynamoDB-only locking to S3-only locking.\n\n> **Note:** In a future Terraform minor version, DynamoDB-based locking will be deprecated and S3 native locking will become the standard. Plan your migration path accordingly.\n\n---\n\n## Testing Your Migration\n\nFollow these steps to validate a successful upgrade from v1.9.0 to v1.10.0:\n\n### 1. Update Your Terraform Version\n\n```bash\n# Verify the installed version\nterraform version\n# Should show: Terraform v1.10.x\n```\n\nIf using a version constraint in your configuration, update it:\n\n```hcl\nterraform {\n  required_version = \">= 1.10.0\"\n}\n```\n\n### 2. Address Breaking Changes First\n\nBefore running any Terraform commands:\n\n1. Update all S3 backend configurations to remove deprecated root-level IAM attributes (see [S3 Backend Changes](#1-s3-backend-removal-of-root-level-iam-role-assumption-attributes))\n2. Update any `moved` blocks that reference resource types with reserved keyword names (see [`moved` Block Changes](#2-moved-blocks-required-resource-prefix-for-reserved-type-names))\n\n### 3. Re-Initialize\n\n```bash\nterraform init -upgrade\n```\n\nThis re-downloads providers and validates the backend configuration.\n\n### 4. Validate Configuration\n\n```bash\nterraform validate\n```\n\nAddress any errors or warnings before proceeding. Warnings about deprecated attributes (like `dynamodb_table` or `shared_credentials_file`) can be resolved at your discretion but are not blocking.\n\n### 5. Run a Plan and Review\n\n```bash\nterraform plan -out=upgrade-plan.tfplan\n```\n\nReview the plan carefully for:\n\n- Any **new `(sensitive value)` annotations** \u2014 these are now correctly marked; verify they are expected\n- Any **new resource changes** that were not expected \u2014 these may have been masked by the previous mark propagation bug\n- Ensure no resource **replacements** are proposed unexpectedly\n\n### 6. Apply the Plan\n\nIf the plan looks correct:\n\n```bash\nterraform apply upgrade-plan.tfplan\n```\n\n### 7. Verify State\n\n```bash\nterraform show\n```\n\nConfirm that the state reflects the expected infrastructure state.\n\n### 8. Test Sensitive Value Behavior (if applicable)\n\nIf your configuration uses sensitive variables with conditional expressions, verify the behavior:\n\n```bash\nterraform plan -var=\"secret_value=test-value\"\n```\n\nConfirm that sensitive values appear as `(sensitive value)` in the output where expected.\n\n---\n\n## Rollback Guidance\n\nIf you encounter issues after upgrading to v1.10.0 and need to revert:\n\n### Prerequisites Before Upgrading\n\nBefore upgrading, take these precautions:\n\n1. **Back up your state files:** Copy your state files to a safe location before running `terraform apply` with v1.10.0.\n\n   ```bash\n   # For local state\n   cp terraform.tfstate terraform.tfstate.v1.9-backup\n\n   # For S3 backend \u2014 enable versioning if not already enabled\n   aws s3api put-bucket-versioning \\\n     --bucket my-terraform-state \\\n     --versioning-configuration Status=Enabled\n   ```\n\n2. **Record your current state version:** Note the state serial number with `terraform show -json | jq '.values.root_module'` before upgrading.\n\n### Rolling Back the Terraform Binary\n\n1. Reinstall Terraform v1.9.x:\n\n   ```bash\n   # Using tfenv\n   tfenv install 1.9.8\n   tfenv use 1.9.8\n\n   # Or download directly from releases.hashicorp.com\n   ```\n\n2. Revert your configuration changes (restore deprecated attribute usage in S3 backend, remove `resource.` prefixes from `moved` blocks if needed).\n\n3. Run `terraform init` to re-initialize with the older version.\n\n### Rolling Back S3 Native Locking\n\nIf you enabled `use_lockfile = true` and need to roll back:\n\n1. Ensure no Terraform operations are in progress (check for `.tflock` files in your S3 bucket).\n2. Remove `use_lockfile = true` from your backend configuration.\n3. Run `terraform init` to re-initialize.\n4. If any `.tflock` files remain in S3 from a failed operation, remove them manually before running Terraform again:\n\n   ```bash\n   aws s3 rm s3://my-bucket/path/to/state.tflock\n   ```\n\n### Rolling Back State If Needed\n\nIf a `terraform apply` with v1.10.0 produced unexpected changes:\n\n1. Use `terraform state` commands to inspect and correct state:\n\n   ```bash\n   terraform state list\n   terraform state show <resource_address>\n   ```\n\n2. For S3 backends with versioning enabled, restore a previous state version:\n\n   ```bash\n   aws s3api list-object-versions \\\n     --bucket my-terraform-state \\\n     --prefix path/to/my/key\n\n   aws s3api get-object \\\n     --bucket my-terraform-state \\\n     --key path/to/my/key \\\n     --version-id <version-id> \\\n     terraform.tfstate.restored\n   ```\n\n3. Push the restored state:\n\n   ```bash\n   terraform state push terraform.tfstate.restored\n   ```\n\n### Important Rollback Caveats\n\n- If you used ephemeral resources or variables during the upgraded run, rolling back to v1.9.0 means those features will no longer be available. You must refactor configurations back to use `sensitive` variables.\n- The `assume_role` block syntax for the S3 backend is supported in v1.9.x. If you have already migrated to the block syntax, you do not need to revert to the deprecated root-level attributes.\n- S3 native locking (`.tflock` files) is only understood by Terraform v1.10.0+. If rolling back to v1.9.x, ensure any lock files are cleaned up first.\n\n---\n\n## Summary of Changes at a Glance\n\n| Change | Type | Action Required |\n|--------|------|-----------------|\n| S3 backend root-level IAM attributes removed | **Breaking** | Must migrate to `assume_role` block |\n| `moved` blocks require `resource.` prefix for reserved type names | **Breaking** | Update `moved` blocks if affected |\n| Sensitive marks propagate correctly in conditional/for expressions | Behavioral (bug fix) | Review plan output for new sensitive annotations or unexpected changes |\n| Ephemeral resources, variables, outputs (beta) | New Feature | Optional; adopt for improved secret management |\n| S3 native state locking via `use_lockfile` (experimental) | New Feature | Optional; consider for migration away from DynamoDB locking |\n| `ephemeralasnull()` function | New Feature | Optional; use with ephemeral values |\n\nFor further help, see the [official Terraform upgrade guide](https://developer.hashicorp.com/terraform/language/upgrade-guides) or start a discussion in the [Terraform community forum](https://discuss.hashicorp.com/c/terraform-core).\n",
